“HE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On icceipt at Ihe Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps (of any province) equal to the price stated
(which is published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
Some Recent Books.
East Lynne, Mrs. Henry Wood’s best-known
book, is now published in a neat cheap
edition ; cloth, 2s 4d.
Beyond the Dreams of Avarice, Walter
Besant’s new novel, belongs to the respect-
able family class of fiction. The old
motives and situations are furbished up
again, and there is a nice, satisfactory end-
ing. Cloth, 6s.
The Indiscretion of the Duchess is a short
story by Anthony Hope, of no particular
interest or value. Paper, Is 3d.
The Ralstons, Marion Crawford’s last book,
is a sequel to “Katherine Lauderdale,”
and has rather more interest, though the
writer does not reach the level of his
early books. Paper, 2s lOd ; cloth, 3s lOd.
The People of the Mist, by Rider Haggard,
takes one again into strange and adven-
turous country, and the story goes with
some swing and vigor. Haggard is out-of-
date nowadays, but those who admired
King Solomon’s Mines should also admire
The People of the Mist, which is brightened
by many illustrations. Paper, 2s lOd.
The Wonderful Mission of Earl Lavender,
by John Davidson, is a prose fantasia
which will seem rather foolish to the aver-
age reader, and not very wise to the
adept. Earl Lavender certainly has a
grand idea for a new Evolution, which
enables you to eat and drink freely at
your neighbor’s expense ; but the applica-
tion of the idea is made none the easier
by the Earl’s experience. The feature of
the book is the description of a London
society of flagellants, weary of natural
interests, and seeking an unnatural
stimulus in the pleasant dissipation of
whipping one another’s bare backs. Cloth,
6s 6d.
A Journalist’s Note-Book, by Frank Frank-
fort Moore, is a book of gossipy reminis-
cences, with many good stories new and
old. The price (cloth, 6s lid) is rather
high for a book of ephemeral interest, at
all events from the Australian point of
view.
Corrected Impressions, by George Saints-
bury (cloth, 7s lid) is a collection of
literary appreciations by the English critic
who perhaps of all others comes closest to
the pinnacled Saint-Beuve. Thackeray,
Temiyson, Carlyle, Swinburne, Macaulay,
Browning, Dickens, Matthew Arnold, Wm.
Morris, Ruskin, and “ three mid-century
novelists” (George Eliot, Charlotte Bronte,
and Anthony Trollope) are all cleverly
treated ; and the book has many grateful
thoughts for a lover of literature.
Cheap Fiction.
Well-printed ; neatly bound in cloth ; 2s
each.

ley.

Some Books Worth Reading.
In the Midst of Life, by Ambrose Bierce.
Boards, 2s 6d. Some wonderfully fine
short stories.
The White Company, by Conan Doyle,
Paper, 2s lOd. A good historical novel.
Memoirs of Sherlock Holmes, by Conan
Doyle. Paper, 2s lOd.
Adventures of Sherlock Holmes, by Conan
Doyle, Paper, 2s lOd,
A Study in Scarlet, by Conan Doyle. Paper,
Is 3d.
The Sign of Four, by Conan Doyle. Paper,
2s lOd. First-class detective stories.
The Woman in White, by Wilkie Collins.
Paper, 9d ; Cloth, Is. The anchor’s
best book, well printed.
The Cloister and the Hearth, by Charles
Reade. Paper, 9d ; *Cloth, Is.
Tess of the D’Urbervilles, by Thomas Hardy.
Paper, 2s lOd. A masterpiece of English
fiction.
Crime and Punishment, by Fedor Dostoieff-
sky. Paper, 3s, One of the most re-
markable of Russian realistic novels.
The Anarchist Peril, by Felix Dubois.
Cloth, 6s 6d. A startling exposure.
Lorna Doone, by R. D. Blackmore. Cloth,
3s. A charming English romance.
Looking Backward, by Edward Bellamy.
Paper, Is 3d. The bane or the antidote.
Pictures of the Socialistic Future, by Eugene
Richter. Paper, Is 3d. The antidote or
the bane.
Studies in Pessimism, by Schopenhauer.
Paper, Is. 3d. Delightfully cynical.
The Green Carnation. Paper, 45. ; cloth,
6s. 6d. Witty and amusing.
Yirginibus Puerisque, by R. L. Stevenson.
Clcfch, 6s. 6d, Essays perennially charm-
ing.
My Lady Nicotine, by J. M. Barrie. Cloth,
6s. 6d. A book chief among ten thousand
and altogether lovely.
In a Canadian Canoe, by Barry Pain. Cloth,
4s. Sketches, verses and stories varying
in merit, but with keen thought and a
clever style at the back of all.
Prose Fancies, by Richard Le Gallienne.
Cloth, 6s. 6d. A recent book of idyllic
essays.
Post-Prandial Philosophy, by Grant Allen,
Cloth, 4s. Readable essays.
The Autocrat of the Breakfast Table. The
Professor at the Breakfast Table. The
Poet at the Breakfast Table. By Dr.
O. W. Holmes. A beautifully-printed
edition ; neatly cloth-bound, 2s. lOd. each.
Also, pocket size, paper covers, 9d. each.
The Manxman, by Hall Caine (cloth, 45.).
A really fine novel, epic in breadth and
intensity.
The Tales of John Oliver Hobbes (including
“Some Emotions and a Moral,” “A Study
in Temptations,” “A Bundle of Life,”
and “The Sinner’s Comedy”). Piquant
and clever. A charming edition, with
portrait of author ; cloth, 6s. 6d.
Aut Diabolus Aut Nihil and other stories.
Cloth, 4s. Clever and strong.
A Child of the Age, by Francis Adams.
Cloth, 4s 4d.
Kenelm Chillingly, by Bulwer Lytton. His
best novel; cloth, 4s Id (recommended) ;
also, cloth, 2s 6d.
John Bull and Co. Cloth, 3s. lOd. Max’s
new book about Australasia and South
Africa.
Esther Waters, by George Moore ; cloth, 6s.
6d. A strong and remarkable story.
The Story of Francis Cludde ; cloth, 3s lOd.
Under the Red Robe, by Stanley Weyman.
Paper 2s lOd.
A Gentleman of France, by Stanley Wey-
man. Paper, 2s lOd.
My Lady Rotha, by Stanley Weyman.
Paper, 2s lOd.
Four clever and interesting new historical
novels.
Ships that Pass in the Night, by Beatrice
Harraden. Paper, 2s lOd; cloth, 4s.
Novel and clever.
Paradoxes, by Max Nordau. Paper, 3s.
Acute, fresh, thoughtful essays on many
interesting subjects.
National Railways, by James Hole. Cloth,
5s 6d. A full, instructive and interesting
discussion of the State railway question.
Papa, Mamma, and Baby, by Gustave Droz
(English translation). A witty, naughty,
delightful book, which should be first upon
every list of wedding presents. Paper, Is
9d ; cloth, 2s 9d.
Labour and the Popular Welfare, by W. H.
Mallock. A clever and instructive essay,
from the anti-socialist standpoint. Limp
covers, Is 4d.
Isis Very Much Unveiled, by Edmund Gar-
rett. A scathing exposure of the Mahatma
forgeries ; paper, Is 3d.
The Yellow Book, the new unconventional
magazine, established for the express pur-
pose of giving Mrs. Grundy tits. Yols. 1,
2,3, 4 ; cloth, 6s 6d each.
American__Authors.
“The David Douglas series of American
Authors ” is charmingly printed, and the
books are small and compact, suitable for the
pocket. Posted at la 3d each volume, paper

Rudder Grange is a marvel of fun, and in

By Geo. W. Cable : —Madame Delphine ;

very Newest Woman.

Subtly, shamelessly audacious.
One Fair Daughter, by Frankfort Moore.
Cloth, 4s 3d. A book about wicked women
by a wicked man ; smart and lively, and
full of naughty little thrills.
The Story of a Modern Woman, by Ella
Hep worth Dixon. An insidiously vicious
attack on modern men. Cloth, 4s.
Discords, by George Egerton. This is a
companion volume to “ Keynotes,” and
unquestionably strong, though the con-
tinual stress on the same sex-note grows
monotonous. As for the “ wickedness”-
the candour of the intimate references to
married life (in “Virgin Soil,” for ex-
ample) has rarely been matched outside of
medical works. Mr. Besant’s Young
Person shudders, no doubt; but she reads
all the same, and the book sells furiously.
Cloth, 4s 4d.

A BIG BIZI
Our Trade is Big 1 Our Store is Big !
Our Ideas are Big I
We get bigger every day; on Saturday nigbt
we are bigger than on the previous Monday,
and on the Wednesday following we are bigger
■still. We can’t help growing. We have all the
elements of growth. The soil we are planted in
is good, the climate propitious, the people kind.
We are continually watered by the lifegiving
showers of a nation’s approval. Can anyone
wonder that we grow?
Our growth is a wonderful thing. People
describe it as magical. There is no magic in it.
Anyone, or any set of people, can do the same if
they do as we have done. But they don’t; they
can’t find the way, charge too much for the privi-
lege of doing business with them, and generally
and deplorably make a mess of the whole affair.
That’s the mischief.
Most People Like Us! All Believe in Us!
Not a Pew Swear by Us!
It’s cupboard love. We suit them, make their
money go a long way, and generally render the
path of life an easy one. There’s no sentiment
about it. It’s real biz, and no mistake. In that
way we are beloved throughout Australia, the
measure of affection each one bestows upon us
being gauged by the amount of benefit received.

‘A GOLDEN SHANTY.’
Their Last Death .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Last will and testament of Jessie Perkins, aged
about 35, who committed suicide by suffocating
lierself with the fumes of charcoal at South Mel-
bourne, Victoria, A.u. 1895 :
Blessed Father, loving Saviour, I humbly ask Thy
loving- mercy and pardon for the deed I am going to do.
I have not been able to get any work to do. I have not a
penny in the house, and am deep in debt; and to see my
darling aged mother turned out into the cold street with-
out any money, and not to have any place to put her dear
head on to—oh, God! it is more than I can bear! Blessed,
loving Saviour, it is with an aching heart that I write
these few lines, and with eyes that can scarcely see for
tears. Dear Lord and Saviour, have mercy on our poor
souls. Pity and protect us, and receive our poor souls
into peace'and comfort; and may we meet my dear angel
brothers in Heaven again. Dear Lord, have mercy upon
us, and may Thy blessed Spirit have mercy and grant us
that blessed mercy and heavenly grace, and that loving
pardon that we do humbly pray for. Have mercy upon,
and may our souls rest in peace. Amen.
Hear this weird refrain repeated up to Heaven, as
it swells;
Hear the quaint, pathetic echo of the merry New
Year bells:
“ Saviour mild, who meekly journeyed with Thy
cross to Calvary,
I am woeful, wistful, weary—take Thou now my
cross from me.”
Hark ! the howling of the demons in the near and
nether hells,
Cursing Earth and earthly patience in the clang
of New Year bells,
Claiming souls of shrieking men-kind, shrinking
souls of women fair.
Claiming souls of starving women, dumbly driven
to despair !
Hark! the prayer for peace and pity, faintly,
fondly, how it swells
Up to far, eternal Justice, ’midst the clang of
New Year bells ;
Hear the strange, weak, plaintive pleading of this
creature with its god,
Hear it? Yea ! and may it smite us in our blind-
ness like a rod !
Hear it? Yea ! and may they hear it —hear, and
pale before it, too,
When the great awakening coraeth and the rifle-
shots ring through ;
Hear it ?—No ! they will not hear it, ’tis too faint,
and far and small,
These were only “common” people who have
suffered —that is all !
Common folks ; a common story, save the end,
which smacks of tears —
Many die, but e’en as they did —maybe one in
many years.
Busy world—which needs sensations—drops lip-
pity as it reads.
Passes on to “ stocks and markets,” life hath also
other needs.
First the bank, which failed, and left them —
genteel paupers—in the slums.
Glad to toil for Dives, thankful for the largesse
of his crumbs,
Glad to sell that vassal service—just as glad as
you and I
Are to vend our souls and bodies when we find
the one to buy.
Turned to weary music teaching—here is fitting
food for laugh,
Let them write the satire largely on the self-
.slain’s cenotaph—
How her whole discordant being, doomed in dark-
ness, aye to be,
Quivered ’midst the chords harmonic of some
master’s symphony.
“Times grew bad and teaching failed her” —runs
the modern legend—“ ’till
She was forced to making lollies and to vend
them.” Still
Spectre Hunger mowed and gibbered, closer,
closer yet it drew—
Then the last despairing effort and the mortgage,
and the Jew !
“ Merry Christmas ” came and left them in their
proud seclusion there;
“ Happy New Year ” rose and mocked, them from
some far-off revel where
Food and wine were freely lavished, lights were
glancing, laughter fell—
Echoed dream of distant Heaven wafted down
through space to hell!
Then they turned to one another—aged mother,
aching child,
Spoke of “ Death ” and “ Sin ” and “ Duty ” and
the codes by man compiled
For his soul’s express salvation, for his Ego’s
future good,
When his house becomes a grave-pit and his
raiment rotting wood.
What was Death that they should dread it, food-
less, loveless, hopeless, proud ?
What to them the narrow dwelling and the coffin
and the shroud ?
What had that to add of torture or uncertainty or
strife,
When each sun that climbed to zenith glittered
down on Death in Life ?
Glittered down at last on Silence—gleamed from
golden dawn to gloom,
Still on Silence ! Silence ! Silence ! in that dark,
close-fastened room;
Glittered down, oh God Eternal, morn and noon
and haunted night,
On a Something, still and tragic, on a gaunt and
ghastly sight:
Glittered down while men and women in a laugh-
ing, living tide
Flowing out and flowing inward, swept the level
pave outside —
Glittered down but not to gladden, or to sadden,
or to touch
Two at least of all the thousands who have
“ sinned and suffered much.”
Hear, again, the message pleading through her
anguish and her fears,
Penned to Him—who also suffered—when “her
eyes were full of tears ” !
Hear again the dying daughter of a mother doubly
dead,
Praying God’s eternal justice that they “ might
be comforted ” !
Hark the quaint, pathetic echo of our merry New-
Year bells,
That from out “a land of plenty” up to God
Almighty swells ;
“ Blessed Father, loving Saviour, hear my last
fond, fervent prayer,
Debt and Hunger ! Pardon, Father— it is more
than I can bear.”
Sydney, Feb. 18, 1895.
EDWIN J. BHADY.

preventative posted in sealed letter, 1/2 stamps.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This o ffer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

SATURDAY, APRIL 6, 1895.
The New South Wales Local Government
Bill.
That Local Government Bill for which New
South Wales has been patiently waiting
the last thirty years or more has at length
come to hand, and the province’s reward is
by no means commensurate with its patience.
For a generation, or thereabouts, the public
men of New South Wales are understood to
have been pondering over this great question.
It has been constantly before them all that
time, and, meanwhile, Queensland, Victoria,
Maoriland and other provinces had Local
Government systems of their own in active
operation, and the politicians of the old,
slow-going mother-province had every oppor-
tunity to study these, and discover, by the
experiences of their neighbors, how to con-
struct asystem which would combine all the
best, and none of the worst, features of those
already in existence. Tne result of so much
study and so much valuable example should
have been something better than the average.
Therefore, it is disappointing to find that
Premier Reid’s Bill is about the weakest
and most unfinished affair of its kind
that could well be imagined. The things
that are in it are altogether remarkable, and
the things that are left out of it are still
more remarkable, and the whole scheme
vaguely suggests that it was compiled in
haste, with a broken pencil, on the back of
an old envelope, and then thrown on the
table of the House to take its chance, what-
ever that might be.
• * #
The Bill itself con tainsa comparatively small
part of the scheme. Most of the essential
provisions are embodied in a series of bye-
laws by which it is accompanied, and which,
being apart from the Bill itself, cannot be
amended in any way by Parliament. The
measure simply gives power to the Governor-
in-Council to issue regulations at his own
blessed discretion and that of the Cabinet,
which regulations shall have the force of law
and shall cover at least three fourths of the
whole subject, and Parliament is asked to
sanction all these regulations in advance—
whatever they may be. Also, when the
various Local Government bodies are formed,
each one of them is to have the power—sub-
ject to the approval of the Governor and the
Cabinet—to alter these regulations as it shall
see fit. The regulations deal with the mode
of electing councillors, qualification of coun-
cillors, qualification of electors, collection of
rates, method of keeping accounts, expendi-
ture of funds, audit, and many other sub-
jects. By giving each local body power to
amend this code of by-laws, therefore, it
may come to pass that in one district the
first elected councillors may be all rich land-
lords who will forthwith proceed to enact
that none but landholders shall be eligible
for membership. And, meanwhile, in the
district alongside, the council may be
swamped by trades-unionists, who may so fix
things that only the horny-handed son of toil
shall have a say in the management of affairs.
The Local Government bodies are not only
to administer the law, they are also to make
a new law for themselves ; and they will pro-
bably keep diverging from the original plan
until there are 40 different Local Government
systems instead of one. No province in its
senses wants so much Local Government as
this, nor does it want to inflate the local
authority into a law-maker. The average
local authority is sufficiently inflated as it is.
* # #
Among the principal objects of Local
Government are the relief of the public
revenue by causing all purely local works to
to be executed out of local rates, the aboli-
tion of the “department trotter,” and the
extinction—or partial extinction—of that
nefarious system by which the Ministry is
enabled to buy the support of the electorates
with roads and bridges and culverts and
gaols and similar “benefits.” Therefore, it
is depressing to notice that Premier Reid’s
Bill does not promise to relieve the general
revenue, that it furnishes new and glorious
facilities for the same old “department
trotter,” and that it leaves the gate open for
the same old system of Ministerial bribery as
heretofore. Under the existing Act, general
rates may be levied up to Is. in the £ on the
annual rental value of property within the
municipality, and special rates not exceeding
Is. in the £ may also be levied, making a
total of 2s. Also, it provides for an annual
endowment from the national Treasury of
20s. in the £ for the first five years after the
municipality is established ; of 10s. in the
next five years, and of ss. in the succeeding
five years. After fifteen years the endow-
ment is to cease entirely, and, in the case of
many of the N.S.W. municipalities, that
period has long since expired. The new
Bill provides that general rates may vary in
the case of shires from a minimum of half-a-
farthing to a maximum of three farthings in
the £ on the unimproved value, or from a
minimum of 6d. to a maximum of Is. 3d. in
the £ on the annual value. The minimum
in the one form of rating is thus one-sixth in
the maximum, and, in the other case, two-
fifths, and one minimum is more than twice
as large as the other. A special rate is per-
mitted to the same extent. In the case of
municipalities, the same general rate is pre-
scribed, but no special rate is definitely
provided for, and, as very few municipali-
ties can keep solvent on a tax of Is.
3d. in the £, serious trouble may be
expected. Also, all municipalities, dis-
tricts, and shires are to be endowed indis-
criminately, beginning at the first-class
borough which is endowed for 15 years
certain, at a rate which begins at 10s. in the
£, down to the fourth-class shire which is
endowed for 30 years certain, and for the
first five years receives 60s. in the £ per
annum on all rates collected.
« * *
Under these circumstances, Local Govern-
ment promises to be expensive. There is no
provision in the Act to define what is a
fourth-class shire or any other class of shire,
or to distinguish among first, second, or
third-class municipal districts, or first or
second-class boroughs. Therefore, every
shire will try to be a fourth-class shire, and
every municipal district will do its best to be
a third-class municipal district, and every
borough will use its utmost endeavours to be
a second-class borough, so as to get the
largest possible endowment. And, as the
settlement of these matters is left entirely to
the Ministry, the road-and-bridge politician
will button-hole the Government in the
good old way, and sell his vote to get his
own district put in the class which gets the
biggest endowment, and the corrupt or time-
serving Minister will buy support by shifting
the district which votes straight into the
best-endowed class. Also, the proposal to
lavishly endow first-class boroughs is pure
foolishness. The allowance of 10s. in the £
to be paid to the cities and 15s. to towns,
most of which are already incorporated, is
not shifting a burden from the national to
the local revenue, but transferring a great
part of the burden now borne by the local
rates back again to the State Treasury. A
certain amount will be saved by compelling
the poor and remote parts of the province
to incorporate themselves and pay a part of
their own charges out of local rates, and the
amount of this saving will be wasted in
endowing the richer districts on a vastly
more lavish scale than heretofore. This is
the ordinary process of local government
turned upside down, and is cumbersome
and ridiculous to boot. The inhabitants of
the empty West are to be endowed,
partly at their own expense, and partly
at the expense of the city population,
and the people of the cities are to be
endowed, partly at the expense of the
remote country districts and chiefly at the
expense of themselves. Also, the necessity
for the endowment arises greatly because
the land-tax, which is the chief legitimate
source of revenue of the local bodies, is
limited to a ridiculously low figure, and it is
so limited, apparently, because the Govern-
ment proposes to tax the same land a second
time for the purposes of the national
revenue, and the amount raised by this
second land-tax will have to be handed
back to supplement the deficiencies of the
first land-tax. All of which is a fearful and
interesting complication.
# # *
The new Bill is full of curious anomalies
which should gladden the hearts of many
lawyers in the hereafter. The attached
regulations provide for a plural property
vote on a large and most objectionable
scale, the maximum allowed being four
votes, and the Bill itself provides a severe
penalty against anyone voting, or attempting
to vote, twice at one election, quite regard-
less of the fact that a right to vote four times
is distinctly permitted. The Bill provides
for the division of the province into boroughs,
municipal districts and shires, and furnishes
elaborate definitions of the two latter, to-
gether with rules for their establishment and
government, but leaves the boroughs wholly
undefined. Further, it provides that all the
unincorporated portion of the province shall
be at once formed into shires, and a few
pages further on it declares that any part of
it may be formed into municipal districts.
It provides for the endowment ot boroughs,
municipal districts, and shires, and sets forth
that the receipt of the President of the shire
shall be a sufficient acknowledgment when the
money is paid, but there is no provision where-
by a borough or municipal district can give a
satisfactory receipt, so these latter will collect
their endowment with considerable difficulty.
Also, the Act purports "to repeal certain
words in section 168 of the old Municipali-
ties Act, which words are not there, and
some confusion is likely to arise in con-
sequence.
* • *
Probably the most hopelessly foolish pro-
viso in the whole Bill, however, is that
under which any 20 ratepayers may require
that a general vote shall be taken on almost
any subject under the sun, the cost of the
proceedings to come out of the local revenue.
In a large municipality, so small a num-
ber as 20 aggressive or obstructive per-
sons can always be found to keep on
demanding a general vote on all manner
of subjects, either out of pique, or mere
cussedness, or desire for advertisement,
and this proviso promises endless worry
and expense and public aggravation. The
number ought at least to bear some fixed
ratio to the ratepaying population. Also,
whereas the old Municipalities Act pro-
vided that local bodies might borrow to
the extent of five years’ rates only, which
was certainly sufficiently lavish, the new one
authorises borrowing to nearly twice this
limit. Another serious drawback is that
Premier Reid’s great work, so far as some
of its clauses are concerned, is an altogether
new Act ; and in some other clauses it
simply provides that certain words shall be
struck out of the old Municipalities Act and
certain other words inserted in their stead ;
and in other parts again it provides that
certain clauses shall be lifted bodily out of
the old Act and inserted bodily in the new
one. In consequence of which and other
circumstances it is clumsy and incomprehen-
sible, and likely to lead to much contention
and litigation. Altogether the great measure
which New South Wales has waited for so
long is'a woeful disappointment now that it
has arrived.
Principle or Plunder.
In New South Wales, at present, the pro-
verb that “ hungry dogs will eat dirty
pudding ” is revoltingly illustrated by
aspirants for office. Politicians are for once
betrayed by their desperate necessities, their
paltry ambitions, or their inflamed vanity, into
casting aside the decent garments of alleged
fidelity to principles with which they ordinarily
clothe and veil their true natures, and are
exhibiting the depravity of their minds naked
and not ashamed. The Outs are rolling over
each other in squalid plots to assassinate
the Ins, and have burst into public view',
clutching and scratching one another in furious
disagreement about the division of the antici-
pated plunder. The public owes them, for this
enlightening exhibition of themselves, no com-
mon debt of gratitude. This spectacle of the
once apostle of Freetrade feverishly stipulating
that he shall be leader of a gang, organised to
cut the throat of the cause, cannot fail to im-
press the public with the unreality and spuri-
ousness of the most solemn pretences and
asseverations of political conviction. Almost
equally enlightening is the anxiety of alleged
Protectionists to waive all attempts to replace
the paltering tariff of to-day by one truly
effectual—in consideration of being admitted to
a share in the spoils of official position. The
poet Gray neatly defines the successful methods
of a conqueror : “to wade through slaughter to
a throne.” In narrower spheres of action the
same spirit prompts men to more contemptible
exploits. They are not ashamed to wade
through disgrace to a portfolio. The country
quivers with mixed emotions of scorn, amuse-
ment and indignation at the revelations of
conspiracies baulked hy the irreconcilable greed
of the plotters. People learn with advantage
that so long as the differences to he harmonised
were confined to mere considerations of aban-
donment of principles, everything proceeded
smoothly, but that there was no reconciling
the pretensions of the bandits when it
came to arranging the apportionment of the
plunder. The Freetrade plotters were amiably
willing to permit the existing tariff to continue
for an indefinite period—a tariff" which they
have repeatedly and constantly for over three
years denounced as inflicting the most direful
injuries upon the people of the province. Pro-
tectionists who have, on the other hand, fre-
quently declared that the tariff is ineffectual to
diffuse the benefits of Protection and prejudicial
to the public appreciation of the quickening in-
fluence of a truly Protective system, have been
equally willing to allow the country to remain
tantalised by the bogus thing.
• # #
Summarised, the situation stands thus : The
ex-chief Freetrader is willing to abandon Free-
trade—for office. Sundry alleged Protectionists
are willing to abandon effectual Protection
for office; and rank and file on each
side are eager to work together—for office.
The conspiracy only breaks down because
there are not offices of profit sufficient to go
round. There are two claimants for every
position, from that of Premier to that of whip.
And, inasmuch as it is requisite to hoist some
colors other than the black flag of mere political
buccaneering, the colors of Federation are to be
elevated. Parkes, who denounces Reid for
insincerity in respect of associating with him-
self in the Cabinet Mr. Want, one of the group,
of pronounced opponents of the Federal move-
ment, publicly invites to his arms men of all*
parties on the sole ground of agreement in,
desiring to get rid of the present Cabinet as,
incompetent. Among the allies thus Sought is,
to be numbered an antagonist of Federation,
just as pronounced as Mr. Want, in the person,
of Mr. Crick.
* * *
Those Protectionists who have shown a readi-
ness to accept the proffered leadership of Sin-
Henry Parkes have a distinct advantage over
the insurrectionary contingent of Freetraders
who have lent countenance to the coalition
plot. The former, at least, would secure the
perpetuation of the present tariff. The latter,
pledged to the eyes to destroy this tariff, would
sacrifice everything which they had professed
to regard as essential to the prosperity of the
community. But what malign influence afflicts
the Protectionist cause that it should be doomed
always to entrust the direction of its political
guidance to a Freetrade renegade ? First, the
shifty Dibbs —one day “a Freetrader of the
Freetraders,” the next day a convinced Protec-
tionist ; one day a swaggering advocate of an
Australian Republic, the next the humble
recipient of a title of knighthood from the
Sovereign of Great Britain and its “ de-
pendencies.” Next, the shifty Parkes,
scarce rested from exhorting the people
of New South Wales to rise and em-
power him to sweep away every vestige of
Protection, and now inviting them to thrust
contemptuously aside the “wretched fiscal
squabble.” Only the other day claiming
the eternal gratitude of the people for his
achievement in extinguishing a feeble five per
cent, ad valorem revenue tariff, and now declar-
ing his willingness to save the country by
averting the destruction of a tariff rising to
fifteen per cent, ad valorem, and even higher ir
respect of specific duties.
# # •
Meanwhile, the situation of Ministers,,
has become decidedly less strong with,
the lapse of time. The fact appears tc.
be that while they continue to obey tjie.
impulse derived from the electors, a,, con,,
siderable and very influential contingent o| those,
who issued the mandate have changed, their-
minds. Till recently the wealthy orders—the.
commercial magnates and the owners of reali
estate—have been, almost unanimously, actively,
antagonistic to Protection. The system,
appeared to them to involve interference with,
the profits of importation. Bankers, merchants,
(only another name for importers in New South,
Wales), money-dealers and landowners com-
bined to throw the full weight of their
antagonism into the scales against Protection,.
To-day they have begun to realise—or to fancy,
they realise—what Freetrade, carried into«
practice, really involves. They learn, aghast*
that repeal of the ineffectual tariff"—ineffectual!
as regards stimulating local industry—must
involve taxation drawn from their own
pockets. They are Freetraders no longer.
They are not indeed Protectionists. They
are simply Revenue Tariffists, and as such
they beat the floor with their hoofs and make
noises with their gullets when Dibbs presents
himself at city banquets, while they receive
the Freetrade prophet Reid with chill reserve.
# # #
There can be no alliance between these;
gentry and honest Protectionist or honest Free-
trader. They have no genuine fiscal con-
victions. They are distinctly Conservative
and Fatman, and otherwise are unclassifiahle.
Their aim is to maintain conditions which shall
keep the burden of taxation upon the backs of
the masses, and prevent an ounce of weight
from being imposed upon their own shoulders.
Their defection may undermine the Reid
Cabinet. Already the Chamber which is all
their own—the Legislative CounciL-is prepar-
ing to destroy every measure of property-taxa-
tion presented to it. The greedy faction of
office-hungerers, who hope in the confusion to
possess themselves of portfolios, can only do so>
by serving the selfish aims of this class, now
actively organising, through the machinery of
the National Association, resistance to direct
taxation. If the Protectionists do not ener-
getically bestir themselves they will discover
that they have been betrayed into an alliance
with the Conservatives.
# • «
The Democracy of New South Wales may be
divided in opinion with respect to Protection
and Freetrade. But, with regard to the inci-
dence of taxation, it can never be divided by
honest conviction. Those are bogus Protec-
tionists who pretend that their principles in-
volve any repugnance to direct taxation. The;


genuine Protectionist can join hands with the
democratic Freetrader in agreement that the
expenses of the State, the maintenance of law
and order, the improvement of communications
and harbors and rivers, and bo on, must be
defrayed by citizens in proportion to the benefits
they derive from such outlays, as nearly as
legislative ingenuity can devise methods for
apportioning burdens. The ideal can never be
achieved in perfection. There is no such thing
■as perfection possible in this world. But the
ideal Protective tariff is one which produces no
revenue whatever. -The true Protectionist
.statesman would constantly strive towards that
■condition. Thus, so far as revenue-raising is
■concerned, theoretical Freetrader and theoretical
Protectionist meet on identical ground. NoCus-
itoms revenue. Consequently, direct taxation.
* * m
The true policy of Protectionists to-day
iis quite irrecoucileable with the office-greed
■or financial desperation of necessitous or
lax-principled politicians. The existing tariff
has, notoriously, inflicted infinite discredit
inpon the Protectionist cause. It has created
mo appreciable employment. It has called into
(existence no industrial establishments. Away
with it, at all hazards ! Better that the
jpeople who feel instead of reasoning should
learn by bitter experience what a free-port
ireally signifies to them. For, although this
stariff has not created much employment, it has,
ito some extent, maintained it. Let the people
■of New South Wales realise what it means to
fhave the sugar industry strangled and the
Hast plank drawn from under the feet of the
ifarmers. Let Mr. Reid and his followers ex-
jperieuce the unpopularity which always re-
wards those who impose new taxation. Pro-
rbably the worst foes of Protection were the
Hast Ministry. The deadliest to Free-
(trade will be the present—if the Protec-
itionist theory be" correct. If such prove
■to be not the case; if under Freetrade
(the country be prosperous, so be it. What
matter the means so long as the end is attained?
Two years of open ports should cure the com-
munity vf all hankerings after more of the same
experience. The great desideratum is an
honest, downright trial of one system or the
other. The Dibbs Ministry dared not, or
cared not to, give a trial of genuine Protection,
Neither of the two preceding Parkes Minis-
tries dared or cared to venture on absolute
Freetrade. If Mr. Reid has the courage of his
opinions, for Heaven’s sake, and for the sake of
fiuality of some kind, let him try.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
More About the Federal Balance-Sheet
The trouble re the financial aspect of Federa-
tion still rages in the daily press, and the old
flails keep on pounding out the same old straw
unto seventy times seven. It lias now been
shown several dozen times over that it is prac-
tically impossible to prove anything in the
matter that is worth proving, but beyond that
very little has been done. The financial aspect
of Federation only becomes more chaotic with
prolonged consideration. Of the Australian
(provinces, Queensland has the heaviest, and
■Victoria tiie lightest debt, and they both want
tthese matters to be duly “considered.” Wes-
itralia lias the largest area of unsold lands ;
(Queensland claims to have the largest area of
■unsold cultivable land, and S.A. puts in a
similar claim; the other provinces have very
■little pubi c estate. Therefore, some provinces
(Would like to throw the State lands into the
(Federal treasury, and others wouldn’t. S.A. has
The only payable railway system, but its returns
■ are diminishing, and it promises soon to have a
■ deficiency. Some of the other provinces have
■insolvent railway systems which are progressing
itowards solvency, and others have insolvent
■systems which are progressing towards worse
insolvency. Some of the postal departments
have an increasing deficiency, and others have
a deficiency which is steadily diminishing,
while one—that of S.A.—shows an absolute
profit. The abolition of border customs and
the establishment of a uniform railway tariff
will enable Victoria to annex much of the trade
of southern New Soutli Wales, and South
Australia to take some of the trade of western
Victoria, and Queensland some of that of
northern New South Wales ; while the latter
province and Westralia, so far as present
appearances go, would not annex anything. All
these and several dozen other things have been
proved so often that they have become ancient
liistory. Also, the critics of Federation want
to know how an equitable system can be devised
for the distribution of surplus revenue among
the different provinces ; how the Federal
Government can be prevented from show-
ing gross favoritism by building wild-cat rail-
ways (say) in Queensland, or Westralia, or
the Northern Territory, which will become a
burden on the other provinces; how the separate
provinces can be prevented from piling up new
debts after their old liabilities have been taken
over by the Commonwealth ; and various other
conundrums. The obvious answer to which, of
course, is taat the surplus—if there is one—will
■ be apportioned in the best way possible—or,
perhaps, in the worst way—but that no abso-
, lutely perfect system can be devised ; that the
■ Federal Government will build new railways
where it “darned well pleases;” and that if the
1 provincial Governments want to borrow on their
■own account they will most likely do it. Also,
that nobody is likely to be fully satisfied with
■ the Federal arrangement, no matter how the
■finances are adjusted, and that every time the
Federal Government builds a new railway in
•one province the other provinces will curse as
'to the manner born, and there will be an ill-
Hsed feeling generally, which provincial poli-
ticians of a certain class will encourage to the
best of their ability. Further, au effort
is evidently going to be made to have it
enacted in the Federal Constitution that no
protective duties shall be levied beyond a cer-
tain fixed limit, and probably another effort
will be made to secure the insertion of othe.
provisoes regarding excise, land questions, the
referendum, &c. If these matters are embodied
in the Constitution there will be much trouble,
and there will also bh much trouble if they are
not embodied. All of which only goes to prove
that the Federal Constitution won’t be perfect,
that it can’t please everybody, and that it can’t
even wholly please anybody. And the sooner
these facts are generally understood the better.
The Bulletin, for one, expects to be able to
pick out a score of glaring iniquities in the
Federal Constitution when it arrives, but still
this paper wants it to arrive, and to arrive as
quickly as possible.
Honor Among Sharks.
Editor Bulletin, —If it is your privileged
custom to include all lawyers in one iadis-
criminating anathema, I don’t complain. If
you will, every advocate is a devil’s advocate;
every solicitor is a maw, and no more. Grant
that we are cheeked like ironclads and billed
like pelicans; that we devour the portion of
the widow and the fatherless; that our sole
value is as aids to faith, because there must be
a hell to put lawyers in. Call us moral Adams,
naked of principle and unashamed ; parasites
who give Justice an itching palm; brigands,
bandits, vultures, harpies, sharks. But,
between shark and shark, don’t deny our
etiquette of voracity; between buzzard and
buzzard, our esprit de corps. Sydney Smith
declared that when a churchman is wounded
his brethren stamp with their feet and push
with their horns, until they demolish the dis-
senter who did the mischief; but when a
lawyer is hurt the rest of the profession sur-
round him and put him to deal h. How does
this smart and shallow saying tally with the
generous consideration shown by Bench and Bar
for the feelings of a defaulting Gent. One ?
No ; ours is an honorable kind of thievery.
Though we diddle all men, we love the
brotherhood ; though we jeer God, we
honour the Ring. This fidelity to the
Brazen Rule of “ Fleece and let fleece ”
is so generally conspicuous that lawyers admit
with grief the need of Parliamentary inter-
vention, as by you advocated, in the matter of
Australian Judges whose relatives practise
before them. You argue that a legislative bar
to the administration of justice en famille would
be a public good : I know that it would remove
a professional evil. The public good is nothing
to me; but I want to seize my own good where-
ever I can find it, and Judges’ relatives bar
every path to success. Say lam a barrister.
When I choose a Court to practise in, I know
beforehand that the Judge’s sons or other rela-
tives will absorb (say) two-thirds of all avail-
able briefs. Rightly or wrongly, solicitors
think (or their clients think, which is much the
same thing) that the litigant who briefs the
Judge’s son has one leg in—or afoot, at any
rale: hence the undeniable fact that Judges’
relatives practise chiefly before their relatives,
and that they get far more briefs than their
comparative merit or standing warrants. Well,
I choose. In my first case, lam opposed to
(say) the Judge’s son or nephew. Do you think,
with the knowledge that the Judge can,make
or mar my career tingling through my head,
that I can talk to his sou or about his son
either before his face or behind his back as I
would to or about an unrelated advocate?
Suppose the sou weak, or his case. Dare I
ridicule him when he blunders, or pull a rotten
case down about his ears, when I know, or
imagine, that for every point I score against him
a point is, in the ordinary human course of
things, scored against me by his father ? If
the Judge chooses, and even if he does not
choose, he can ruin me, and that without lifting
a finger against me. He can blast my chances
(unless I happen to be a man among a thousand,
and of the stern stuff of which a Martin, or a
Salomons, or a Pilcher, or a Purves, is made)
with the flicker of an eyelash or the curl of a
lip. Let the whisper once get abroad among
the attorneys and their clients that Mr. So and-
so is an advocate unpalatable to Judge What-
his-name, and I may as well quit Judge What-
his-name’s court at once. So, when a Judge’s
sou is opposed to me, I may have sometimes to
choose between killing my case and killing my
career. The alternative is intolerable ; and the
relative-scandal is only tolerated in Australia.
In England, the swamping of special courts by
barristers claiming close kinship with the
Judge is a thing undreamt of. Whenever
attempted, the practice has been crushed
at once by the emphatic reprobation
of profession and public. Sir Richard
Webster, Attorney - General in the last
Salisbury Ministry, declared in 1891, for ex-
ample, that for a son to practise regularly
before his father was improper and opposed to
the traditions of the Bar (Law Times, June 13,
’9l). The Law Times —perhaps the most
widely-known law-journal—the Scottish Law
Review, and similar organs of professional
opinion, condemn as “objectionable,” “ano-
malous,” and “extremely objectionable” the
examples of family justice which sometimes
crop up in the minor courts. The attention of
the House of Commons was called to such cases
on July 31, ’9l, when the Attorney-General
reiterated his previous expression of opinion,
and stated that he had counselled a son who
called upon him for advice not to practise
before his father. These facts are conclusive
proof of the sentiment of the English Bar.
What that august body of buccaneers would
say were an attempt made to stuff superior
courts with Judges’ relatives, as courts are
stuffed in Australia, I have not the vaguest
idea. But I have a very definite idea what it
would do.—Yours, Shark Junior.
Hud ion. Bros.’ Strike.
The strike at Hudson Bros.’ workshops,
Sydney, is a national affair. If the men are
defeated the whole’commuuity is defeated, the
whole community will be drawn an inch nearer
la misfire —that state of utter degradation in
which human beings, brutalised by toil, exist
and spawn and die on the minimum of food
which will support life ; the whole profit of
their industry being sucked by their employers.
Hitherto, in spite of financial “depressions”
■i i I trade crises, Australian working conditions
have been kept at a rational level of comfort.
The next few years will witness a terrible
struggle to decide whether that level can be
maintained, or whether the “inexorable” law
of supply and demand is to crush Australia also
into the deepest pit of labor slavery. Hudson
Bros.’ strike is an affair of outposts—it con-
cerns directly only 300 men j but it concerns
indirectly every white working-man in the
country. If the rate of wages is definitely
lowered by one firm, other firms must follow
suit; if lowered in one trade, every other trade
may reckon on sooner or later reaching the
same proportional level; if lowered in one city
or province, every other city or province will
answer to the pressure, and the local or “ tem-
porary ” reduction will by-and-by become
general and permanent. Capital is never
satisfied; has never enough; cries “ Give,
give ” as continually as the daughters of
the horseleech. Like stored water, it seeks
always the weakest point; and once it has an
outlet of an inch it pushes its vantage until
every barrier is destroyed. The more it gets,
the more it exacts. For 12 mouths Hudson
Bros.’employ£s have accepted wages 10 per cent,
less than their fellows were receiving from other
firms for similar work. Their reward is an order
to accept a further reduction of 15 to 20 per
cent. Such low r wages for similar skilled work
have, so far as The Bulletin is aware,
never before been offered in Australia. And
— mark this ! —Hudson Bros, are largely sub-
sidised from the public purse. Nearly three-
fourths of their work has always been
Government work—mainly railway carriage-
building. Of some .£20,000 worth of contracts
now in hand, probably £12,000 worth is to the
order of Government—to the order of the
people. This work is tendered for by various
firms on the basis of current rates of wages.
Hudson Bros., it would appear, have already a
10 per cent, advantage over their competitors,
and they ask for an advantage of 25 and 30
per cent. Plainly, Ritchie Bros., Yale and
Sons, the Atlas Engineering Co., Mort’s Dock
Engineering Co., and a host of minor firms,
must follow suit if the men are not enabled
to resist the firm’s demand. The result
will be that engine -fitters, say, who three
years ago were receiving 10s. to 12s. a
day, who are now receiving Bs. to 10s. a day,
will be reduced to Bs. to 6s. 6d. a day ; and so
proportionately with all trades. Hudson Bros,
will not be a whit better off, for there will be
just as much competition at the new rates as at
the old. The province will save money—its
railway carriages will be built more cheaply—
but at the expense of a permanent lowering of
the standard of living, a permanent lessening of
the slender margin of comfort which divides
free men from slaves. The Bulletin says
advisedly that the people of New South
Wales disdain the sacrifice. They have no
wish to grind the souls out of the
bodies of their servants. What Hudson Bros,
desire to do is to take from their workmen the
difference between the wages on the higher
scale—on which tenders for existing contracts
were presumably estimated—and the wages on
the lower scale—on which it is sought to com-
plete the contracts. This difference is to pay
dividends swallowed up in the past by unduly
high management expenses. If Hudson Bros,
succeed in this, the people and Parliament
should see that the firm’s existing contracts are
the last public contracts it handles in New
South Wales.
Pbemier Turner, like every other Victoria ll
Premier since the Coalition days, came into
office with a mandate from the nation to re-
trench, and keep on retrenching to the bitter
end. Yet he justly complains that every in-
dividual act of retrenchment is opposed to the
last extremity, not only by the parties
concerned, but by all manner of people
who are not concerned, and who bear
a hand on the understanding that they
will get similar assistance when wanted.
The latest trouble is at Maryborough. Mary-
borough has a “first-class gaol,” a large,
expensively-managed affair, to which criminals
from all the adjacent districts are sent. But,
owing to the falling-off in crime, there is now
a good deal of spare accommodation in the
Melb. prisons, and the staff is parti-
ally unemployed. Therefore, it is pro-
posed to send all the long-sentence prisoners
from the Maryborough district to Mel-
bourne, and make the local prison a “second-
class gaol ” for purely local purposes, with
a smaller staff. And thereupon a Mary-
borough deputation interviewed the Govern-
ment with extreme ferocity, and demanded
that the “status ” of the gaol should,be main-
tained, wdiether it was wanted or not. Ap-
parently, either Maryborough must have a first-
class gaol, where hangings and things like that
can come off, or else it will do its little share
to down Turner at next election. The moral
is obvious. Every Premier has a mandate to
retrench the other fellow, but when he goes
beyond that and proceeds to retrench this
fellow his end is drawing near.
The New South Wales National Ass under-
takes to prove that the proposed land-tax will
be “ a crushing impost on the producing
classes,” or something to that effect :
If Id. in the £ is all that will be imposed as land-value
taxation, what has the small landholder to complain
about from the actual point of d;w of payment? The
man with £475 worth of land would only pay £1 19s. 7d.,
and he would make an actual saving on the amount paid
through the Customs. Bat he would lose, and will lose,
even now, as every other landholder will, immediately on
the.imposition of the tax, by the decrease in the value of
his property. What does Mr. Garment, actuary of the
A.M.P. Society, say on the subject? “If a tax of only
Id. in the £ were imposed, then on the basis of 4 per
cent., the selling price of an ordinary conditional pur-
chase valued at £1 per acre would be immediately re-
duced to 17s. lid. per acre.” That is to say, each owner
of £475 worth of land will immediately lose £49 9s. 7d.
upon the value of his property, which will affect him in
the long run far more than the mere payment of Id. in
the £.
The two long, hairy ears of the Ass stand out
boldly against the landscape in this pronounce-
ment. The point, as it concerns the “ pro-
ducing classes,” depends, not on the market
price of the land, which is always a fluctuating,
unstable quality, but on the net value of the
produce which can be extracted from it. The
land which produced a profitable crop of
potatoes when it was saleable at £1 an acre will
produce exactly the same crop, other things
being equal, when it is only saleable at 17s. lid.
per acre. Also, if it rose to £3 10s. an acre, or
if it sunk to 2s. 6d. it would—again with the
proviso that other things are equal—yield
exactly the same return. If the State put d.
tax on the land which reduced its selling value
to 2s. 6d. an acre, and spent the money iri
making good roads and railways whereby the
farmer could get his products cheaply ami
readily to a market, it is quite probablfe that
the “ producing classes ” would be better off at
that figure than when the land was worth 20s;
The producer, while he is a producer and not a
land-jobber, doesn’t sell his laud and often
doesn’t even know whether it would fetch 30s.
an acre or 17s. 6d. If the fall in the value of
land is caused by low prices, exhaustion of the
soil, or want of roads, railways, or markets,
then he is affected as a producer ; if the fall
arises from none of these causes, then it affects
him in no degree whatsoever. It is the pro-
fessional landholder—the jobber and the man
who lives on rents—who will be troubled by
the new departure, and it is this person whom
the Ass is so badly worried about, only it hasn’t
the courage to say so.
Brisbane Worker's description of Quigley,
the white Mongreland leper who caught the
infection in childhood from a leprous Chinaman
who lived near his parents’ house :
At the present time Quiolev’s face is puffed and cor-
rugated, his eyes sinking somewhat deep into his head,
and very red. There is practically no hair on his face
either in the shape of eyebrows or lashes, and his com-
plexion generally is of a dull, s dlow hue. Here and there
are black lines—which might readily be taken for dirt,
but which are veins—and these add to the repulsiveness
of his countenance. His ears are twice the normal size,
the lobes being swollen to an extraordinary degree. His
neck, chest, and body are plenteously covered with repul-
sive sores. His finger-tips and parts of his hands are like
so much raw beef rendered putrid and dotted here and
there with blue coloured matter. The same may be
said of his toes, and both hands and feet are swollen
in manner similar to his face. Each finger (which
is eaten away to the depth of the nail) and each
toe are bound up with bandages, and to further hide
his horribleness the hands are encased in fingerless
leather gloves. He appears to be gradually losing his
voice, for he now speaks incoherently and at times his
utlerances are unintelligible at the outset. He can always
make himself understood, but he by no means speaks
with that fluency which characterises the speech of a
healthy individual. Two years ago ho was not nearly so
bad, and contrasting him with ins condition then we
should say that it would be a kindness to allow him to
quietly quit a life which must be a veritable hell.
The 11-years-old white boy recently sent to the
lazarette is afflicted with the same type of
leprosy. Two other cases have been since dis-
covered, and the Mongreland total is now not
far short of 100. Known and isolated—that is;
for no one knows how many unknown lepers are
at large, since the horrible disease apparently
takes any period up to 50 years to develop.
Presently the whole white community will be
anxiously feeling its ear-lobes when it awakes
in the night.
Editor Bulletin, —You suggest democratising the muni-
cipalities. How can that be done more than is done
already? Presumably the ratepaye.s know what aider-
men they want. Do you propose abolishing voting
according to property while you maintain taxation
according to property? You say “monopolies such as
gas-works and tram-lines would be acquired and woiked
for the benefit of the inhabitants.’’ Now, your par. refers
to Vic., of which I know little ; but let me speak of New
South Wales. There has, during the last eight years or
so, come about an entire change in the class of men
offering themselves for election as aldermen in nearly
every country municipality. The change has been, in
nearly all cases, from the level-headed bn-iness man to the
man whose sympathies and interests Inn e been, or should
have been, with the hard working-c uss s whose lot, up to
the time of his election, he shar >l. He was familiar
with the daily life of the people, wim the “shifts” and
devices to which they had to resort in order to eke out
7s. a day (which was until quite recently the common
wage in all country muni ipalities). Yet such aldermen
are always found on the side of what they call “retrench-
ment,” which means a reduction of wages and salaries all
round; itnevermeansthe use of caution in the expenditure
of money on what are called “ public works.” There are
few tramlines in the country towns ; but during the last
eight years many gas-works have been bought by the
local authorities, and many works have been built—with
what result? One result, I admit, has been a general re-
duction in the price of gis to the consumer, but it has
resulted very badly for the poor devil of a gas-stoker, the
gas-engineer, the accountant, and all others obtaining
their livelihood from the manufacture of gas. Salaries
a id wag s are subject to an annual reduction of 10 per
cent. — ‘the democratic alderman” looks upon this as
“his duty to the ratepayers.” Tne question whether the
work performed is worth the wage paid for it is never
considered. The men are usually told that “ there are
any amount of men ready to do your work at the reduced
wages ’’—and they perforce submit. As an instance of
what municipalised gas-works usually come to: Some time
ago the Molong municipality advertised for “ tenders ”
from persons willing to run the local gas-works, “ and
find all labor necessary.” What is this but “sweat-
ing ” ? Anyhow, it is not what The Bulletin
would advocate on “democratic” grounds, is it? An-
other instance: a Hunter River borough recently adver-
tised for applications from persons competent to fill the
posit! in of council clerk, borough overseer, and inspector
of nuisances. “ Preference will be given to persons having
a knowledge of gas-engineering.” They might also, in
the interests of “ strict economy,” have given a further
preference to persons willing to assist as road-laborers.
Will The Bulletin kindly say whether such action on the
part of democratic bodies should be imitated by the coun-
try as a whole? There is not a public company or mono-
poly in the State which has to stand so much comment as
the Australian Gaslight and Coke Company, yet that com-
pany pays its workmen higher wages than any munici-
pality does for similar duty. lam tired of “ democrats”
and their democratic ways. Give me the “ bloated capi-
talist he, at any rate, will pay me what lam worth to
him, and, when he does go in for “ retrenchment," he
generally begins by sacking those who don’t earn their
screw.—Yours, A Half-Roasted Gas-Stoker.
[There is a difference between true democracy
and false. If this country isn’t breeding the
right kind—the kind that wishes to get and to
pay a decent living-wage (and something over,
when possible)—then the idea of democratising
the municipalities is chimerical. It is in the
belief that only the minority of ratepayers are
tarred with the Molong and Hunter River
brush that this paper wrote and writes. Until
a lot more evidence is adduced to the contrary,
The Bulletin refuses to believe that the pre-
sent state of things can’t be bettered—or that
we haven’t got the men to better it. This paper
will take great pleasure in showing up all
attempts at municipal sweating. —Ed. B.]
When Yic. Gov. Brassey’s father was
railway-contracting there was hardly a country of
the world in which he did not take some big job.
At one time he had in all 80,000 men employed
on works involving .£17,000,000. Between 1859
and 1863 he, by his agents, built the Great
Southern, Great Northern, and Great Western
lines of N.S. W. For this work were imported
2000 laborers from England and Scotland, appa-
rently because the local labor available was too
dear and drank too much beer. The 2000 immi-
grants cost the contractors .£5 and the Govern-
ment £l2 per man to land. Seventy-eight miles
of Queensland railway were constructed under
the same auspices. When the old man died he
left .£7,000,000.

My Dear Moorabinda —
It has been definitely decided that Lady Duff
will end her melancholy Australian experience on
13th prox., and, with her daughters, will leave
for Europe, per R.M.S. Victoria, on that date. I
know of no one who does not feel very kindly
towards Lady Duff, and though her official term
was brief she was on the high road to achieve a
certain sort of sober, motherly, common-sense
popularity that will keep her memory green in
Australia. Coming after the glib, but nervous
talkee-talkee “ M.E.J.,” the silent Lady Duff—
who always deputed some flattered male or other
to respond for her, and who, at first, never even
opened a flower-show except per medium of a he-
official—was a not-unpleasant novelty.
People who knew them well seem to greatly
esteem the Duff girls, too. For example, they had
a brave, almost boyish way of “ bearing a hand ”
and not standing on their dignity at picnics and
other junketings when emergencies occurred and
help was needed. There was one hoof-and-wheel
party across country, I remember, when the vice-
regal trap got bogged, and the Y.R. demoiselles
literally put their shoulders to the wheel to help
it out of the mud.
Poor Governor Duff ! He is the first Governor
to be interred in a N.S.W. cemetery, and the con-
sequence is that the vice-regal burial has turned
“God’s acre” into Snob’s acre, especially since
the body of the late Governor will not be taken to
Europe (despite the widely-circulated rumor that
Mr. Pennyman had taken it with him) but is to
remain here. The Waverley cemetery has always
been a fairly fashionable place to leave your poor
clay in, but now gilt-edged prices are, I believe,
the rule, and anybody owning an unoccupied little
lot near the vice-regal portion finds himself a
lucky (grave) digger likely to have an embarrass-
ment of offers from moribund fashionables who
want to be “of the vice-regal party ” even in the
Valley of Death. Some enterprising speculators
are already putting up their little lots with
special stress ou the fact that they are “near the
Governor’s grave.” By the way, Sydney Women’s
Literary Club lately debated “What is a vulgar
person?” Well, I think that a vulgar person is
anyone who utilises the dead for advertising pur-
poses.
Victoria’s new Governor comes out in his well-
advertised yacht, the Sunbeam —the yacht in
which his first wife died, and in which, I believe,
lie went for a honeymoon raid with, Lady Brassey
No. 2 —but I think the Melbournians would rather
be came per balloon which might explode after
landing him, and cut him off from civilisation.
As I predicted, weeks ago, that yacht is going to
lie a sore trial to Toorak. It is already hinted
in the press that Lord B. hopes to make a
holiday trip to Maoriland in the Sunbeam, and
means “to explore the famous Sounds;” and if
his imminent Ex. is planning private picnics thus
far ahead before the full horror of the representa-
tive citizen has been spread before him, and before
the representative eitizeness has tried to call bis
wife “my dear,” what will happen when he knows
the worst? If the Victorian Government has the
true interests of Toorak at heart, somebody will
be hired to scuttle the Sunbeam.
Lord Hopetoun has departed in the odor of
popularity, promising to come out again, just as
did his ex-Ex. Garington, who hasn’t shown up
here since, and isn’t likely to. Apropos of popular
Governors, a man said to me the other day :
“ Why shouldn’t any Governor be popular? I
have to pay 14s. a gallon duty on my whisky ; the
Governor gets his duty-free. If I could get my
whisky for 18s. a dozen, as the Governor does,
instead of for 385., where is the man whocould get
ahead of me in popularity ?’’ From what one sees
of Government House buffet-scrambles this strikes
me as a most reasonable view.
Talking of Governors reminds me that a very
pretty and charming girl, who went ’Ome osten-
sibly to marry a particularly well-connected young
officer of a sponge-oakey complexion, will, so says
rumor, shortly return to the bosom of her family a
blossom still unplucked by the rude hand of man.
I am told that the first lesson the average Aus.
tralian girl needs to learn at ’Ome is that it’s
better business to make love to the average Eng-
lishman’s parents than to himself. People attri-
bute the breaking-off of the match to the machi-
nations of a certain voluble and influential Coun-
tess ; but, however that may be, the young lady is
so truly bewitching, even in the eyes of her own
sex, that it must be her own fault should she ever
lack a large assortment of the bravest and most
eligible of suitors.
Apropos of an Anglo-Victorian match broken
off in London by the somewhat elderly bridegroom-
elect the year before last, they say the free-and-
easy Australian manner “ does for ” many of our
girls in stiffly-starched England. The olive-
skinned beauty concerned, who failed to hook her
big fish, is said to have horrified her should-have-
been relations-in-law with her off-hand habits.
At lunch, one day, for example, a fly got into her
glass of water and she dipped it out with her
spoon-handle and flung it on the carpet ! But
don’t you think that the Australian servant is
responsible for the damsel thus deftly helping
herself out of a difficulty? In Australia you have
to dislodge foreign matter from your victuals for
yourself—there is, generally speaking, no pow-
dered flunkey hired for 10s. per week to fall
forward with a silver salver and a soup-ladle and
save the situation.
Last Saturday was the first day of the “Inter-
provincial Bicycle Championship Meeting ” on
Sydney Association Ground, which has a very
combed-up look just now, owing to its having
been so carefully polished for the international
cricket-boom. The little garden in front of the
members’ stand, which used to be sucli a bob-
tailed floral interval, is now in smart order ; and
if only people with a quiver-full wouldn’t bring
all their hostages to the Association Ground and
set them running amok round the parterres, the
gardenette would be a distinct advantage. As it
is, strangers mistake the place for a day-nursery
or a baby-farm.
There was a large, if rather suburban, attend-
ance on the opening-day, but the dressing on the
members’ was not impressive. We are between
seasons just now, and autumn garments
looked too warm, while the wearers of mus-
lin wore blue noses. Besides, there really
are no autumn fashions until the autumn race-
meeting at Randwick. Far and away the
most lovely object on the members’ stand was
a beautiful youth in a dark blue serge cycle suit
with silver badges, a small blue cap, and a waved
“bang” carefully arranged over his forehead.
Now that women are going in for hard hats and
the rest of it, I suppose Man will take to titivat-
ing himself in order to preserve the picturesque
balance? By the way, a neat blonde girl in
cream sported about nine yards of ribbon match-
ing her Edwin’s colors. As it happened, Edwin
came in with the ruck in every race I saw, and
appeared to be a rather rickety knight for any
girl to barrack for in her trimmings. A really
wise woman would only aggressively flaunt her
Romeo’s chromatic oriflamme when Romeo had a
fair chance of getting his nose in front of his field.
It isn’t kind to draw people’s attention to the
fact that you are the fair ladye of a “ selling-
plater.”
There certainly wasn’t much dressing on the
oval (which looked like golf-links, with the red
flags spotted about), though I don’t mean to say
that an American would have been shocked at the
appearance of the riders. A ’Murkan cyclist’s
wardrobe would make a ’Murkan cycle-meeting a
“men-only” show in Australia, and turn our
riders’ long bare shanks into the very pink of dis-
cretion. Mind you, I don’t personally object to
the bare shins : from a picturesque standpoint, I
think tights would be an improvement in the
more stringy-bark-looking cases, but I suppose
athletes must fly as light as possible—only don’t let
them snicker at the ballet, that’s all. The Aus-
tralian race-track cyclist only peels from his ankle
to a span above the knee—the upper part of him
is modesty itself, if only his garments would
’tend to business—but they won’t. On
Saturday, nearly every rider’s jacket got
loose behind and fluttered in the breeze, reveal-
ing instalments of spinal cord and mysterious
undergarments. That was the comedy of the
afternoon—but it’s to be hoped the wheelmen will
adopt the sensible jockey plan of having a string
run round the hem of the jacket, which ties it
securely round the waist. It is neater, and,
though nobody ever takes a chill while he or she
continues perspiring, it looks more hygienic not to
have a cool breeze playing on the heated spine.
The colors of the cyclists were mostly prettily
mixed. The gallant N.S.W. champion, Meg-
son, of the long horse-shaped face, looked
rather like an In Memorian card on wheels
in his magpie cap and jacket ; and Wyburd,
a reformed footballer, suggested piracy or a bottle
of cold poison with a sknll-and-crossbones in his
black jacket. I didn’t admire a white satin jacket
with infrequent pink spots—it looked like variola;
and though I much bleed Middleton, the Vic-
torian champion’s, willing way with his head in
riding, I didn’t quite fancy his colors—crockery-
blue with a large spotty pattern like a dyed
The success that Arnykos has met with,
and the universa l favour it enjoys both in the Old
and New Worlds, is a sufficient guarantee of the
advantages resulting from its use as a mouth-
wash, gargle, cosmetic, &c. It is unanimously
acknowledged to possess a mild and, at the same
time, energetic efficacy as an antiseptic wat r,
and is superior to all antiseptics at present kno n
to science, as expressed by Professor Sir Joseph
Lister, of Edinburgh, in the Lancet—which alone
ought to make Amykos a household word for
the Australian, who esteems cleanliness. Price,
Is. 61. per bottle at all chemists, perfumers, &c.
hyaena. Nor did I approve of Davis, the S.A.
champion’s, Prussian blue guernsey—it looked
wharf-lumperish among the painted satin suits.
The prettiest of these were a brace of youths who
rode up to the post together, the one in a lovely
shade of apple-blossom-pink satin jacket and cap,
and the other in pale-porcelain-blne satin with
white satin sleeves and blue-and-white-quartered
cap. They looked a pair of china ornaments, and
even if the pale-blue ornament did fall off his
gridiron in the stress of the first lap and lie
doubled up on the track until somebody dragged
him off, he made a lovely little heap of clothes,
anyhow.
But though I talk thus amiably about the cycle
sports I really do not believe in them at all. It
cannot be good to exhaust yourself so that you
tumble off your wheel in a fainting state while
experts pummel you back to life as if you were a
drowned man —and then support you off the field
one at each shoulder, as Middleton was supported.
At these struggles to push a wheel the fastest, one
overhears young men—healthy-looking young
men, too —telling how they knocked off cycle-
racing by the doctor’s orders—how “sick and
giddy ” they felt at the end of a race, and so on.
No wonder Australia is a continent dotted with
doctors’ red lamps when everybody, man and
woman alike, makes a sort of Aunt Sally of his or
her constitution by seeing how many blows it will
take to knock it edgeways. With a growing
generation of pallid female bicyclists and jolted
lawn-tennis girls (now I hear they have a ladies’
football club in London) to be mated with ex-
hausted bicyclists and other played-out athletes—
what a toll the doctors will levy on posterity !
Sydney Liedertafel’s recent concert had a sort
of polite pall over it owing to one or two melan-
choly points being insisted upon by the committee
in deference to the manes of Sir Robert Duff, who
was the society’s patron. The card started
lugubriously with an “In Memoriam,” “The
Long Day Closes,” sympathetically sung Dy the
Liedertafel, who all wore inky neckties “by
order,” and looked like mutes in consequence.
But if the society really meant to give a dust-and-
ashes air to its latest meeting, and to imply that
it only took to the platform to relieve its funereal
feelings, so to speak, it should have insisted upon
the assisting talent (Miss Lottie Hyarn and Miss
Edward Deane, who amiably filled a vacancy)
climbing into weeds too, in order to make up the
platform impressively ; for a battalion of black
neckties cannot hold its own against a soprano in
pink and a piano-expert in mulberry.
The concert was melancholy enough, however,
from a musical standpoint. It opened inauspici-
ously with a disappointment. Miss King, the new
and prominent soprano (I mean “new” in the
recently-discovered sense), did not perform ; and
there seemed to be a sort of mournful sequence
about the items, which began with a dirge and
almost finished with Arthur Deane giving a
gobbled kind of rendering of “ Thou art passing
hence, my brother.” No wonder gloom settled
over the large audience which left the Mausoleum
—I mean, the Centennial Hall—with a depressed,
all-flesh-is-grass sort of air.
The Liedertafel orchestra redeemed the situa-
tion with a most melodious rendering of Gounod’s
“La Colombo.” which ends with a rustling sound
like the fluttering of wings; and a dashing per-
formance of a Spanish waltz. Misses Hyam and
Lucy Allpress—they were scarcely successful in the
Kreutzer, though they got some thrilling effects out
of what Tolstoi calls the wicked part of the sonata
—were best in the selections from “Don Juan.”
There was not.a very fashionable house—“ Court
mourning,” aggravated by Lent, kept the hall com-
paratively free from any big social guns. Judge
and Mrs. Windeyer occupied the serai-circle of
patronage, and the dressing was not enthusiastic.
When you say that almost every woman had a
black bow in her back hair you have stated the
most salient points, though my eye was caught by
a man in a red woollen cap which looked as if
somebody had been sealing his hair on.
I paid a second visit to that most charming and
exquisitely-acted play, “The Case of Rebellious
Susan,” at Sydney Lyceum last Saturday, and
found it going better than ever. You’d think
that Susan would have drawn houses crammed to
the doors night after night, but it didn’t. Yet no
play ever presented in Australia deserved better
houses. The metier of the Australian is, alas!
not sparkling comedy. It is Variety—“ ’E dunno
w’ere ’e are,” with “’E’s found out w’ere ’e are”
for an encore. That, and totes and cigarettes. It
is a dreadful and irredeemably vulgar theatrical
outlook—you cannot expect managers to go on
staging the latest plays to small if keenly-appre-
oiative houses. If I were a despot, the theatre in
Australia should be handsomely subsidised, and
the chief recipients of my Imperial bounty would
be “ the Firm ” and Brough and Boucicault, to
whom this country owes a heavy debt of gratitude
for having supplied it, by means of vast outlays
in money and in labor, with a hundred delightful
and really civilised entertainments.
From David Jones and Co. I have received a
copy of their autumn publication, “ Shopping by
Post.” For country folks I can think of nothing
handier than this manual of styles and prices
thickly sprinkled with artistic illustrations and
showing just how the dresses, cloaks, and hats
look on the figure ; and though I have never seen
a real woman who looked quite like a fashion-
plate—generally a spectre which would frighten a
’bus-horse if it really appeared—still women can
get an approximate idea, anyhow, of how the new
styles look. Also the directions for self-measure-
ment and so forth, contained in “ Shopping by
Post,” must be plain to the meanest intelligence.
Yours affectionately, Sappho Smith.
