A certain amount of audience was attracted
to. Her Majesty’s (Sydney) by the revival of
‘‘Called Hack,” a drama which is past its first
youth and getting well on to middle age. The
plucky Mr. Rignold s version of the story is
chiefly remarkable for its want of resemblance to
the original, and a good deal of interest and
probability have been lost in the process of
adaptation. Pauline, instead of being merely a
girl with a weakened intellect and a lost memory,
is a lunatic, howling piteously in the glare of the
limelight; and Gilbert Vaughan is a babbling
person, who starts out to hunt for a certain Italian
conspirator with fatal intentions, and begins by
telling his story and his intentions to the first
Italian conspirator he meets, so that he may go
away and warn the rest. This blank stupidity
makes the audience feel inclined to throw a large
piece of mud at the picturesque hero. The part
of Paolo Macari has been enlarged till the drama
is almost all Macari, and he comes in on every
possible occasion. When he can find no other
reasonable excuse for coming in, he comes in and
soliloquises about cats, particularly black eats.
Macari is a police spy of intense acumen, and
he cheerfully explains his calling to a man who is
dead certain, in the ordinary course of things, to
give him away and burst up the show. As a
matter of course, the other man does give him
away and bursts up the show. “Called Back,”
as presented by Mr. Rignold, is a good drama,
but it would have been a lot better if some un-
authorised person hadn’t improved the story for
the worse in such a reckless fashion.
• * i
Paolo Macari, with a murky wig and a'
sombre moustache, and a black stubble on his
chin, is a great effort, and Mr. Rignold is hereby
congratulated thereon. The black stubble on his
chin is one of the traditions of the character and:
others like it; the very grim and horrid)
traitor always shaves himself secretly in the dark,
places of the earth, and, in consequence, he never
gets his chin quite clean. Macari’s villainy sticks
out altogether tqo conspicuously, and only the
most simple-minded variety of patriot would be
taken in by him, and he is too reckless to be a>
brainy police-agent, and he has various other
things against him, including his portentous l
stride and his portentous hat, but it is a fine-
performance none the less. Scot Inglis makes ai
manly-looking hero, and the amount of wail which <
the adaptor has put into his part is regretable.
Miss Agnes Knight is a modified success as the-
deranged maiden in white, weeping in the lime-
light. Miss Athena Claudius does the best thaU
is possible with the conventional good girl, andl
Miss Kate Bishop puts a touch of genius into the-
conventional landlady with the spasms and the-
cats and the bottle of gin. Mr. Plimmer is com-
mendable, and Mr. Stirling Whyte is rather less
commendable, and Mr. Cyril Keightly is mainly
conspicuous for the way he opens and shuts his
mouth in moments of excitement. The rest oft
the co. consists principally of ferocious Russians l
knouting prisoners in Siberia.
** * *
That exceedingly pretty comedy, “The
Case of Rebellious Susan,” will last only a
week longer at Sydney Lyceum. “Rebel-
lious Susan” is an effort which grows on the public
appreciation, and looks its best when seen for the
seventh time, or thereabouts. There is no very
definite moral in it, which, in a sense, is a cause
for thankfulness; most stage morals are merely a
burnt offering on the altar of Mrs. Grundy, and a
sacrifice to the god of Commonplace, Therefore,
the audience can make its own moral. If Jim
Harabin, the husband of Susan, was really the
speechless ass he is represented to be by Mr.
Dorrington, then the lady’s conduct was justifiable,
anyhow. If he was rather less of an ass, then her
conduct was more or less justifiable, according to
the exact amount of ass he really was. Also,
when she becomes reconciled to her husband
because her lover forgot her in three weeks and
married another girl, as related by Mr. Compton,
her conduct is fully explained by the depressing
aspect of the messenger, if Mr. Compton ade-
quately represents the messenger. In any case,
“Rebellious Susan ”is a charming play, and the
leading parts are represented in almost perfect
style by Titheradge, Boucicault, and Misses
Noble, Faber, and Temple. Boucicault, as
the maudlin, long-haired dreamer who weds
a stormy, red-headed girl for the sake of
“afflatus,” is one of the most perfect charac-
ters in the piece. The only unsatisfactory
feature is that the s r ory closes without explaining
whether the red headed girl—who ought to appear ■
in pants—got 18 months or five years for inciting
a crowd of females on strike to wreck a post-office.
If the lady’s arrest could be shifted into the second ?
act, and the third act could close with a newsboy
calling out a “speshul edition” with the news :
that she had got five years, it would send the ■
audience away fully and completely satisfied. The .
Bulletin hopes that she gets the five years any-
how, partly on account of the. color of her hair,,
and partly because of the way it hangs dowm
about her ears.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the .manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
poach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.
much paint and limelight in it, and all manner of
magnificence. The present company quits this
transient sphere and goes the way of all theatrical
flesh on Friday night, and The Bulletin hereby
ties a large crape bow on its hat, partly as a sign
of woe at this departure, and partly to conceal the
grease-mark on the front of it.

The ever-welcome Maggie Moore takes
over Sydney Royal next Saturday, and begins
her season with “Little Jack Sheppard,” that
best of all burlesques, which created a furore, or
some similar foreign expression, at the Criterion
years ago, and has been making more or less of a
furore at various other places ever since.
** * *
“Jimmy” MacMahon has closed up the
Haunted Swing at the height of its prosperity, for
the purpose of removing that illusion to more
commodious premises at Sydney Cyelorama, where
it will commence business in a week or ten days.
The Cyelorama comes out presently in all the
glory of a new picture, representing Jerusalem
under certain painful circumstances in its history,
and the combination should do well.

and the O’Rourke and the O’Hooligan, and that
other vast historical character, the O’Higgins.
The new-comer is a success, partly on his own

of Dooley. The other members of the co. still
keep in the forefront of the battle, the Sakers

** * *
The dust of years has gathered on “A
Scrap of Paper ” since the Majeronis last took it
in hand, at Melbourne Bijou, and the unpreten-
tious little thing was not welcomed back to its
old haunt, on Saturday, with much enthusiasm.
“A Scrap of Paper,” in the moral British version
of Sardou’s comedy, is a harmless love-letter,
which an uninteresting married woman wishes to
recover from a former sweetheart. A clever lady
friend acts as ambassador in the matter. She
captures the holder of the note, but when he is
willing to surrender possession of the same it goes
ingeniously astray, and has several narrow escapes
from coming under the notice of the aforesaid
married woman’s jealous husband before
being happily destroyed. “A Scrap of Paper”
was a brilliant novelty in the long ago.
Now it is off - color and out of date. Miss
Amy Roselle, as the ready - witted Susan
Hartley, appears at her very best in one trying
situation, but for the lighter kind of comedy work
her touch is somewhat heavy. Also, Mr. Dacre,
who is not required to do more than bustle gaily
through his part, seems slower and more emphatic
than he ought to be. He has too much manner
and stride, and effusiveness, and he slaps men on
the shoulder far too frequently. For the rest, Mr.
Elton represents an aged collector of beetles with
careful art, and some minor characters are better
filled than others.

“The New Boy” will continue to suffer
much for the sake of his ample Martha until
Tuesday next, when Melbourne will, no doubt,
celebrate the breaking-up of a successful season
in the usual way. On Wednesday and Thursday
nights amateur-ctm-professional performances of
“Pinafore ” are to happen at the Princess’s in the
interests of the Jewish charities. A rush for
tickets set in some time ago, and, according to all
accounts, the sick and indigent Hebrew is bound
to do well by his double-barrelled benefit. If there
be more than one distressed Jew in Victoria they
will both of them do well.

“ Cut Off with a Shilling ” is the new
curtain-raiser at Melb. Cambridge Theatre, where
they have carried “The Amazon” into a third
week. The two men characters in “Cut Off ” are
admirably played by D’Arcy Stanfield and Charles
Berkeley, and Miss Eva Clements is a fair young
bride of the type that bachelors dream about, and
husbands but too seldom see. Meanwhile, the
management is engaging chorus - ladies in readi-
ness for “ Little Don Juan ” at Easter.

On Tuesday next (9th) bonnie Miss Marie
Elster takes her farewell benefit at Melbourne
Royal. The sweet singer will display the quality
of her voice, and likewise prove its powers of
endurance, in three characters. An act of “ Mari-
tana,” the Garden scene -from “Faust,” and
Offenbach’s “ Choufleuri,” with Miss Elster as
the heroine in each instance, are the chief features
of a big bill. Everybody worth mentioning is a
patron or a member of the committee, and every-
body not worth mentioning has bought a ticket in
token of an earnest desire to be present.

The Wild-Cat Column .
The A.M.P. Society, which has hitherto con-
fined itself strictly—a good deal too strictly, in fact-to
ordinary life, endowment and annuity business, issues a
series of tables for new forms of insurance. Some of
these are novel, and most of them are distinctly valuable.
One of the best of the lot is the “ trust investment
policy, under which the society, instead of paying over
the insurance money in a lump sum to the widow, or
other beneficiary, at the death of the insured, pays her
5 per cent, interest during her life, and at her death
hands over the principal to her heirs. By this arrange-
ment a man who insures for, say, £2OOO, can secure for
his widow an income of £lOO a year for life, the £2OOO
benjg paid when she also quits this vale of woe. Con-
sidering that the average widow is fleeced, sooner or later,
by some hard-faced harpy, or loses the money in some
hopeless investment under the advice of a “ friend,” or
is cleaned out by her second husband, who backs
a horse with the pile, the advantages of this
scheme are enormous. The only objection is that
the widow’s heir or heiress is exposed to similar
danger when the money is at last paid over. To obviate
this the society issues a “long-term-annuity policy,”
under which the old man may direct that the society
shall pay the 5 percent, income arising from his policy,
first to his widow, and thereafter to his children, or, fail-
era ’j to h ' s grandchildren, for a period of 50 years.
At the end of the 50 years the income ceases, and the
total amount of the policy is paid over as directed. This
is probably the most complete provision for the future
yet invented. The man who dies with a long-term-
annuity policy grasped in his bony hand can feel that he
has made things reasonably secure for as long a time as
any average dying father cares to look forward.
. Among the other tables are a new plan for
infant insurance; various new forms of annuities; a
scheme by which two people may effect a joint insurance
to secure a fixed income, equal to 5 per cent, on the
amount of the policy, during the life of the survivor, the
whole amount of the policy being paid over at the said
survivor s death ; and various other valuable provisions.
The Bulletin, however, pins its faith to the “ long-term-
annuity ” scheme. The joy of knowing that no crooked
trustee or attorney can get at the money for half-a-cen-
tury certain is something worth dying for.
♦ ♦ ♦
The Mutual Life Assurance Society of Victoria
publishes a balance-sheet which, in most respects, is
eminently satisfactory. The premiums for the year 1894
are £115,000, being about £7OOO less than in 1893, but
this reduction is of comparatively small account. The
funds have increased by £44,000, and are now £902,000
The interest on investments (£44,061) is at the rate of
rather oyer 5 per cent.—a very good proportion under
present circumstances—and more than covers all death-
claims and bonuses. The said death claims amount to
£32,000, and it is a notable fact that they have decreased
every year since 1890. Also, the society has now £26 3s.
Bd. on hand for every £lO9 of insurance in force—a remark-
ably high proportion—and a handsome bonus will be de-
clared next year. One especially good feature in the balance-
sheet is that it distinguishes between interest accrued but
not yet due and interest actually outstanding. The amount
accrued is £709 ; the amount outstanding which fell due
on 30th and Slat December, the latter being the day when
the accounts closed, is £1129 ; and the amount outstand-
ing, which fell due previous to 30th December, and which
can be reasonably described as arrears, is only £370.
The total amount of rent accrued and outstanding—for
some reason this item is not subdivided in the same
fashion as the interest—is only £796, Judging by these
figures the investments must be remarkably sound, for
the first evidence that an investment isn’t sound is
generally the difficulty of collecting interest. The balance-
sheet also shows separately the value of properties ac-
quired by foreclosure, which item amounts to only
£34,000. Altogether, it is about the most definite and
complete publication of its kind yet received, and furnishes
much more information than is usual in such documents.
The expenses of the Mutual Life of Victoria in
1892 were 27Jper cent, of the premium income, or nearly 20
per cent, of the total income. In 1893 they were 28| per
cent, on premiums, or nearly 21 per cent, on total income.
And in 1894 they were 29J per cent, on premiums, or a
little over 21 per cent, on the entire income. Considering
the steady reduction in death claims, the smallness of the
losses on investments, and the good rate of interest
realised, it appears that the society can stand this heavy
expense rate without serious injury, but it is a lament-
ably heavy rate all the same, and the recent increase is to
be regretted, especially at a time when many other
societies have contrived to show a small reduction. The
Mutual Life of Victoria, by the way, is not the
worst sinner in this respect, but it is a bad enough sinner
all the same.
♦ » * *
The Scottish Widows’ Fund makes a big bid for
fame by the announcement that it has £15,000 stand-
ing to the credit of the “ unclaimed surrender value
account,” which it is eager to pay over to the right-
ful owners. Such moneys come under a like category
to the unclaimed balances in banks, concerning which
legislative enactment is being constantly demanded. The
voluntary announcement by the Scottish Widows’ Fund
deserves praise, whether the move is bold advertisement
or only a piece of solid Scotch honesty.
♦ * * *
The Sydney Permanent Freehold, Land and
Building Co. met its depositors, the other day, with a
proposition that the current half-year's interest should be
paid in six instalments extending over three years, that
payment of interest be stopped for two years,’and there-
after be reduced to 2£ per cent., and that the deposits be
repaid at some date not fixed, after all other liabilities
are discharged. The meeting kicked considerably against
these drastic proposals, but finally agreed to reduce the
rate to 2J per cent., the next two years’ interest to stand
over and to be paid when circumstances admit. The
proposal to pay the interest now due in six half-yearly
instalments was dropped. Altogether the shareholders
conceded a great deal more than any reasonable board
had a right to expect, and the fact that only one indig-
nant clergyman proposed liquidation is an evidence that
the modern Australian creditor is a patient and long-
suffering animal.
* • * *
The Melbourne property market is beginning to
be alive again. The South Melbourne Permanent Build-
ing Society held an auction the other day, and actually
disposed of £lB,OOO worth of property. In most oases
there was active competition, and the prices realised w r ere
much better than was expected. Which is more wonder-
ful than the rising of Lazarus from the dead. Twelve or
eighteen months ago the auctioneer might have raved in
his rostrum until he broke a blood-vessel and died, with-
out getting a bid, unless some seedy man with his hair
standing up through his hat had scornfully offered tup-
pence for the lot.

The liquidators of the Melb. Metropolitan
Bank and Metropolitan Building Society announce
that they have realised during the past year
£29,700 by property sales, have paid off £38,000
worth of deposits, and reduced the total liabilities by
£121,000. Since the liquidation began, £234,000 of lia-
bilities have been paid off. They further report in tones
of joy that the total liabilities are now only £350,000, and
prices are improving so much that it seems actually pos-
sible that they may pay off everything and have a balance
left for the shareholders, or even that the dead and gone
Metropolitan may one day start again. Whereat half the
audience was too much stunned to speak, and the other
half uttered the first cheer which has been heard at a
liquidation meeting since 1892.
» ♦ * *
The Liverpool and London and Globe Insur-
ance Co., one of the oldest and safest foreign offices in Aus-
tralia, was established in England in 1836, and founded
its Sydney office in 1853, taking over at the time all the
business of the Australasian, Colonial and General Life
Assurance and Annuity Co. The committee which
authorised this incorporation included such names as
Alfred Stephen, Edward Knox, William Walker, Charles
Nicholson, G. K. Holden, and John Gilchrist. Mr.
Elward Knox was for a long time one of its directors,
while Sir Charles Nicholson, for some time Speaker of
N.S.W. Legislative Assembly, is now Chairman of
the London Board of Directors. Six of its oldest life
policy-holders are or were the late ex-Chief Justice
Stephen, E. Deas Thomson, Thomas, Icely, Mr. Jus-
tice Manning, James Norton, and Mr. A’Beokett. Their
life policies are nearly half-a-century old ; ex-Chief
Justice Stephan’s policies doubled themselves in bonuses,
and five years ago the co. (rave him the privilege of dis
continuing the payment of his premiums. The company’s
assets at the end of ’93 totalled £9,085,790, and during
the last few years of depression the trading balance be-
tween fire premiums and expenditure has reached most
satisfactory figures ; it was £70,544 in ’92, and £70,955 in
'93. In the following clearly-specified terms and figures
the company guarantees profits to policy-holders:
“The assured's share will be nins-tenths of the pro-
fits ascertained to have accrued upon life policies
of their class on the participating scale.’’ Its Aus-
tralian and Maoriland affairs are wholly managed by the
Sydney Board. Nearly £250,000 is invested in Australian
banks ; the current account of the Sydney Head Office is
£50,000, and all Australasian funds are invested in Aus-
tralasia. Since inception to’93 the Australian office has
paid £1,939,500 under fire and life policies. It is now
beginning to push its life business, and for this purpose
is working on a table, which allows the payment of
reduced premiums for the first five years.
* * * *
The latest alleged Customs fraud is said to have
been perpetrated by the simple process of vast ly over-valu-
ing goods exported, for the purposes of drawback. This
idea opens up a new and extensive field of iniquity. For
instance, a firm may import £5OO worth of dutiable goods,
and, by means of a bogus invoice, represent their value
as £2OO, and pay duty on that amount only. Then it
may re-export them to another province, and, by means
of another bogus invoice, represent their value at £lOOO,
and thus get refunded five times the duty it paid. Then
the importer in the adjacent province, by means of a
third bogus invoice, may represent their value as £3OO,
and pay duty on that amount, and re-export them again
with a fourth sham invoice, which represents them as
worth £909, and get his duty refunded three times over.
Until tba goods wear out from actual hard travelling
there wema no end to the proceeding, unless Federation
comes along, and interprovincial duties are abolished,
and there are no more drawbacks to be collected.
Federation will be the end of more than one swindle
which now flourishes exceedingly.
» • » •
That truly painful concern, the Melb. Freehold
Investment Co. (in liquidation), which has probably more
unsaleable property on hand than any other concern of
its size, proposes to make still another effort to recon-
struct. Its last reconstruction scheme is still remem-
bered as the most melancholy proposal ever held out to
any body of creditors in this country.
♦ * * *
There is much grumbling out back (N.S.W.)
among the few free squatters who continue there. They
say that on the banks’ stations, with very few exceptions,
next to nothing is being done to fight the rabbits. Their
own efforts are thus largely defeated ; either their ex-
penses are piled up, or the carrying capacity of their runs
is greatly reduced. To men who have this year got 4Jd.
for the wool which, two years ago, realised 10d., this is a
matter of importance.

cantile world and a church leader. It recalls the two
footpads who stuck up a Scotchman, and, after an
hour’s hard struggle, got him down, and found that all he
had was threepence. “Great Soolt,” exclaimed one of
the footpads, “ if he had half-a-crown he would have killed
both of us.”

How they “have” the Customs. A clothier
when packing goods for drawback (i.e., where refund
of duty paid is claimed on exportation of the goods
or goods made of duty-paid material) surreptitiously
slips various parcels, which have been checked on the
invoice, under the counter. Some few days later he
passesanother entry, claiming refund or drawback a second
time on the same goods, and scores a further profit.
Another game is to say men’s suits made up of 3yds.
duty-paid cloth, are made from 3£yds., and as there is no
practicable check, the State again suffers.
* * ♦ «
The Bears have had their time on the silver-
market, and the price has dropped to an extent sufficient
to frighten History out of its grave. The Bulls are now
having a turn, and an alleged paucity of supplies is
quoted as a reason for a rise of a few sixteenths of a
penny per ounce. The Japanese are said to be demand-
ing £70,000,000 from China. This would be one reason
for a hardening in silver stocks, but the “paucity of
silver” argument is no good. Silver could not go on
dropping in (gold) price for ever, and, at any rate, it
must have some pauses for rest on its way down to its
minimum.
* * * •
Vic. Minister of Agriculture Taverner, estab-
lisher of the minimum wage on Govt, contracts, is npw to
the fore with a scheme for placing the provincial wine
industry on a firmer footing. His proposal is to build
large centrally-situated cellars in the various Vic. wine-
producing districts, whither the smaller vignerons may
send their grape-juice to be treated, graded, and stored
on a large scale. The first winery will be at Rutherglen.
Up to date, N.S.W. has made no move in this direction.

* » * ♦
Sydney Daily Post has gone the way of all
journalistic attempts to exist on the heave-offerings of
Labor and Democracy of its peculiar kind. For twelve
weeks the paper has been run by a very good staff, which,
despite innumerable disadvantages, kept the sheet well
before the public as a newspaper. The first week’s salary
of the literary staff was paid; half of the second week’s
was also forthcoming, then the limp and down-at-heel
ghost staggered on his halting parade and fell right down.
Since 20th Jan. not one of the employes has received a
cent by way of salary, and from the editor, to whom
nearly £lOO is owing, down to the smallest messenger
boy in the office, all are creditors of the company. On
Tuesday morning a demand was made for salary, and was
not responded to. Then the boys struck. Labor omnia
vincil. The Post got about three months’ work out of
its employes for about three weeks’ wages.

« * * «
A scheme of unemployed relief, with much more
brains in it than such schemes usually display, has been
formulated by the “ N.S.W. Co-operative Prospecting and
Mining Association.” Any person with charity and funds
may take shares and help to send out prospectors under
Scientific direction in likely localities. If there is any
loss, it is reckoned in advance ; if any profit, workers and
hackers divide it. Some likely ground has been leased
from Govt. ; and with tools and rations found, the men
Bent out should easily make a living, and may make a pile,

cess for treating Barrier sulphides has to do with the

Men who know the Mt. Brown (N.S.W.) dis-
trict, seeing the dry-blowers at work about Coolgardie,
have resolved to keep the older field in mind. Four or five
have already returned, and half-a-dozen machines are on
the ground or on their way thither. More prospecting is
being done than at any time since the first rush, X 3 years
aeo. But the country, some old diggers think, is little
likely to produce any big things.

Central B.H. shows on last half-year’s work a

the Extended cross-cut. But this profit is scarcely
proper to the half-year. Against it has to be set much-
exploratory work done and paid for in the dark days.
The co. had, at Jan. 31, £14,761 10s. Id. credit balance,
which must have been added to in the last two months,
as the furnaces have been running steadily.

“Billy” Jones has offered, on behalf of an
English syndicate, £576,000 for the Tasmania G.M. and
Q.O. Co.’s property, and, as the directors approve, the
shareholders will probably agree. During the last 17
years the co. has raised 244,390 tons stone for 329,2770 z.
gold, paying diva. £581,475. The capital was originally
£200,000 in 4000 £5 shares, of which £2 12s. is called up.
Amalgamation with the adjoining Florence Nightingale'
and Phoenix raised the capital to £230,000. At one time
shares were as low as 65.; the English price now values
them at £l2. If the sale comes off, Govt, will reap a nice
little sum through the new income-tax.

There has been a sudden development at the
quicksilver mine on the Jamieson River, Vic. The rock,
on which the cinnabar is freely present for the first lime
since sinking on the lode, is covered with quicksilver. It
shows a large percentage of quartz through which the
cinnabar and free quicksilver run uninterruptedly.

The plot of Boldrewood’s
new book, “The Sphinx of
Eaglehawk,” is laid in the year
1852. Yet, on page 28, bespeaks
of “Major Noretusk, V.C.,”
going to a fancy-ball as “a
sowar of irregular horse with a
cuirass of chain-armour taken by
him in single combat in the
Indian army.” Now, the V.C.
was not instituted until '56, so
that the-Major could not have
worn the Y.C. in ’52; and, if
we are to infer that the cuirass
was captured in the Indian
mutiny, that is equally impos-
sible, because the Mutiny did
not break out till ’57. Boldre-
wood should get Louis Becke to
he'p him in his dates.
Since the early days of
Edward Gorrv there has not,
perhaps, been a better 6st. 7lbs.
jockey in Australia than little
Jack Stevenson, who lately
attracted notice by riding three
and four winners in succession
at Flemington and Caulfield.
Stevenson was “ discovered ” in
Sydney by trainer Foulsham,
and taken over to Victoria some
18 months ago. He is still quite
a little fellow—much more of an
infant than his photograph
would lead one to suppose, and
if encouraged to practice the art
of ruling to win, may presently
show the public how great is the
difference between a jockey and
the avtrage pigmy on horse-
back. Stevenson, like many
stable-lads who have come to
the front, is a mild, seraphic-
looking young party — a nice
specimen of the promising light-
weight class. The quieter the
boy, the better for his ’orse, as
a rule.

election.
Eleven years ago, Mrs. Mott, who died
near Mount Barker, S.A., during last Feb., aged
101 years, had these living descendants—9 children,
91 grandchildren, 185 great-grandchildren, and
2 great-great-grandchildren. When she died her
progeny must have approximated to 300.

averages eight hours literary work daily for seven

had a holiday for nearly a decade. One day
shortly a hearse will be seen in the street. Inside

Ex-Premier Patterson, gushing afc the

had budded like a rose, and shed sweet perfume

and from thence diffusing an influence for good
over the whole community.” Atrocious tommy-
rot, of course, but quite a poetic outburst to come
from the dour-visaged ex-meatman. After this
we shall not be surprised to hear a working-
bullock spouting hexameters.

JOCKEY-BOY STEVENSON.
Ex-Gov. Hopetoun and Gov. Brassey are
to “confer” in England at the end of May con-
cerning the government of Victoria. The be-
millioned Brassey will be one more on the list of
Governors who have come expecting to sway the
rod of empire—and have stayed, to be the petty
idol of a sycophantic coterie.
One incident in the
“New Boy,” at Melbourne
Princess’s, thrilled every
bald-head with excited envy.
That is when the pert and
pretty Edith Housley coils her
girlish arms round the diminu-
tive Ralph Roberts’ neck and
wants to kiss him, as shown in
the picture herewith. The New
Boy’s refusal to be kissed is a
distinct breach of the natural
law, and is the most glaring im-
probability Thk Bulletin has
ever noticed in a play.
“Will Honeycomb,” in
Dubbo Despatch, says that the
late Governor Duff had a most
extreme aversion to signing
death-warrants. When the
cases of Montgomery and Wil-
liams, sentenced to death for
wounding a policeman, were be-
fore the Executive, the members
of that body and the Govt, were
anxious to avert the death-
penalty, but the report of Judge
Stephen, who was present and
whose duty it was to sign the
death-warrant, left no loophole.
When Premier Dibbs handed the
Governor the executive minute to
sign, prior to handing it over to
Judge Stephen, H.E. refused,
stating that he held the pre-
rogative of grace and pardon.
The Premier then told him that
on reading the official instruc-
tions he would find that these
privileges had been taken from
him and vested in his Ministers.
The Governor read and re-read,
and admitted that he had no
alternative, signed, flung down
the pen in disgust and left the table.
Chief-Justice Griffith, who 35 years ago
was entered on the roll of students at Sydney
University by his father, Rev. E. Griffith, visited
the University last week to perform the same
duty for his own son. Years ago, it was Griffith’s
hope that Queensland would have a University of
her own long before to-day.
Both the Governor of Pentridge Prison
(Col. Bull) and the Governor of Melb. Gaol (Capb.
Burrowes) want to retire. The real reason, not
given in the newspapers, is said to be that they
are continually quarrelling with the Inspector-
General, Capb. Evans, who governs governors
with a rod of sealing-wax.
At the Melb. “ citizens’ ” banquet to the
vanishing Gov. Hopetoun, H.E. spoke from
voluminous notes (probably outlined by secretary
Wallington), and while he was speaking he
dropped them. Down he dived under the table,
and while he was below someone filled up his
glass. It was like a bit of a farce.

the lawyers of the old legal families

Dr. C. J. Martin, now of Sydney Univer-
sity, will almost for a certainty succeed Prof.
Halford in the chair of Physiologv at Melbourne.
His recent researches on the action of snake-

One difficulty in the appointment of Chief
Justice Dailey as Governor of N.S. Wales is per-
sonal to the Judge. His Honor has served only
about seven years as Chief Justice, not half the
time necessary to entitle him to a judicial pen-
sion. As six years gubernatorial service would not
carry a pension with it, between the two stools the
Acting-Governor would come to the ground in his
old age.
Professor George Britton Halford, M.D.,
Dean of the Medical Faculty, Melb. University,
who has lectured in the Medical School for 33 years,
has just sent in his resignation, advancing three
reasons therefore : “(1) I am an old man of 70;
(2) I do not want to die in harness ; (3) I believe
that an infusion of young blood would benefit the
school.’’ Ceology Professor McCoy, however,
stoutly declares that, for his part, he will remain
in harness to the last.

toun ever had was when a Bendigo miner was
killed under sensational circumstances, and he

embezzlers of banker and politician quality it
should not be hard to get into the right ears a
whisper in favor of pale faced, wrinkled-browed,

RALPH ROBERTS and EDITH HOUR LEY in “ The

poetic, spiritualistic, vegetarian, Cyril Haviland,

Few guilty men were ever less guilty than the
ghost-seeing Cyril, and few dishonest men were
ever less likely than he to be made honest by a
protracted residence in prison.
In all probability Mr. Edmund Barton,
Q.C. (now temporarily on the Bench), will be the
next N.S.W. Supreme Court Judge. No nn re
generally acceptable appointment could be made.
Mr, Barton’s career, including his long term as
Speaker, proves him to be a man of eminently
judicial mind, and possessed of the qualities,
apart from a mere knowledge of law, which are
ever desirable in the President of a Court. It is
interesting to recall that “Toby” held his first
criminal brief in 1872, when he was assigned by
the Crown as junior in the defence of the un-
speakable Lester, the Parramatta River Thug.
“Geographic History of Queensland.’*
It is Mr. Archibald Meston’s misfortune that no-
body has ever been able to take him seriously,
except himself and the photographer who took
the portrait reproduced in this week’s Bulle-
tin. Whether even himself takes him seri-
ously may be doubted, for there is a certain
munchausenish, gasconian twang about his Fgo
which Alter Ego should find irresistibly comic.
On the other hand, the Mestonian “ I ” reaches
such prodigious dimensions that by all the laws of
complementaries “ i ” must be a personage far too
insignificant to snigger. Certain it is, at all
events, that though Mr, Meston has been a con-
siderable figure in the second rank of Queensland
notables for the last 20 years, and has played, not
Photo, by Lomer ] [Brislane.
ARCHIBALD MESTON.
without credit, many minor parts besides those of
pressman, politician and naturalist, his name is
never mentioned without a smile. That his
magnum opus, the just-published “ Geographic
History of Queensland,” will alter the local
appreciation of his character, and transform
him from unveracious humorist into veracious
chronicler, is not so certain. Others than
Jerome have discovered how persistently people
find fun in the pathos of the funny man anxiously
endeavoring to get rid of his label. And Mr.
Meston jokes unintentionally even on his title-
page, for, whatever “Geographic History ” may
mean, it surely does not mean anything like the
contents of the succeeding pages. The book
should really be called “ A Miscellany of Curious
and Interesting Facts concerning Queensland and
Australia.” It has no particular literary merit;
though the author has been unable to deny him-
self the pleasure of reprinting a quantity of
turgid rhetoric collected from the purple patches
of his previous writings, of which one can
only say, with old Abe Lincoln, that
for people that like this sort of thing, this
is just the «ort of thing those people like.
With commendable forethought, however, the
dubious dithyrambics are immediately followed
by a chapter on “ Church Statistics ’’—doubtless
in order that the contemplation of holy things
may shame the critical blasphemer into silence.
But, dithyrambs excepted, the style throughout is
the flattest journalese ; there is no attempt at con-
nected narrative ; and the “ Geographic History ”
merely brings together, tinder a number of inde-
pendent headings, the cream of the information
contained in Mr. Meston’s head, or diaries, or
scrap-books, as the result of a life’s reading and
observation. Mr. Meston has read much and
remembered much ; he has united, as an observer,
exceptional talent to exceptional opportunity; and
his book does credit to his industry in compilation
and his perseverance in original research. Its
shortcomings have been noted ; but they are far
outweighed by its merits. The book is unique,
and every Australian should get it and read it
at once. No other man could have written it;
its facts are little known, and most have
never before been brought together, while the
author adds much that is new and striking of
his own ; it is extremely interesting, and a trea-
sure for reference. Mr. Meston has a real genius
in the collection of materials for history, and The
Bulletin (which takes him seriously) congratu-
lates him on producing a book of extraordinary
and permanent value to Australian literature.
The volume, by-the-by, is poorly printed and
meanly bound by the Queensland Government
Printer (who plainly did not take the author
seriously); and it is to be hoped that a second
edition will soon be available with all the typo-
graphical embellishment that the contents de-
serve.
The word “reconstruction” is a disease
in Victoria since the banks failed. Down at
Nutnurkah, the impecunious council has notified
the shire and water trust officers that their
services will be dispensed with, “with a view to
reconstruction of duties.”
* Geographic History of Queensland. Dedicated to
the Queensland people. Brisbane: Edmund Gregory,
Government Printer, 1895.

berley in W. A. Parliament. It is distant about

the other day, the first time for 10 years. A
week’s trip cost “Alick” ,£lOO.

Wrixon, Yic., back from the Colonial
Conference on Canadian politics. “The Mac-
donald Government was in office for 30years, with
a break of only five years. Political forces in
Canada are more settled than ours.” Settled is a
good word.
Dunedin (M.L.) has started a wax-match
factory. It employs nearly 100 hands, and turns
out good matches at the same old price, and, so
far, it has more orders than it can supply. The
duty of 1/4 to 5/6 a gross is the visible reason
of its success.
The martial Sargood, at a recent Melb.
softgoods ceremony, claimed that “we ragmen
have always come to the front in politics and
social life.” Sargood makes the mistake of think-
ing he has come to the front, whereas he has
simply cultivated it.
The great question of the “ walk-about ”
Kanaka is still agitating Bananaland planters.
The “walk-about ” is that variety of S.S. Islander
who refuses to return to his island, and knocks
about Queensland towns working when he pleases
and making things generally lively in his leisure.
Proposed to compel him to return or re-engage.

Since the Victorian coal-mines were opened
two or three years ago, the price of coal to the
railway department has dropped by nearly 9s. per
ton. Freetraders complain that the Vic. Govern-
ment pays more for Vic. coal than the price at
which it could now procure the superior article
from Newcastle. But the Government patronage
keeps the mines open, and Newcastle coal is cheap
because the Vic. mines are open. If they once
closed, the good old coal-famine days would pro-
bably soon come back.

political difficulties.”— Thorold D.
Chits of boys, as a Sydney constable
points out, are paid Is. lid. per hour for filling up

done indignant Robert is right in his growl.

In view of the awkward predicament in
which Australia’s public men have landed the
prospective Federation, by gushing invitations to
departing vicegerents, it will be necessary, to save
breach-of-promise actions, to put the Governor-
Generalship in commission. Jersey, Hopetoun,
Kintore, and Carington have already been effu-
sively asked to take it, and, of course, Brassey
will be similarly attacked ere long.
The Dominion Goveinment, according to
cables, seems to have stepped in to give denomina-
tional education the place in the Canadian pro-
vince of Manitoba which a majority of the resi-
dents refused it. Manitoba not long ago established
a national school system, which parents of all
religions could freely support; bub the Catholics,
beaten at the local polls, appealed to the Federal
Govt, for aid, which has apparently been granted.
If Chief Justice Darley were selected as
N.S.W. Governor by the Imperial authorities,
Chief Justice Griffith (who practically appointed
himself to the Bench) would no doubt aspire to
the Queensland Governorship. Then (to show the
noxiousness of such a system) we should have the
recreant radical and black-labor apologist as
figure-head of a community by most of whose
members he is politically, if nob personally,
detested.
Gov. Brassey (please pronounce this name
“ Bracy ”) owns 26,000 acres of land in Westralia
which, he says, he bought “for purely philan-
thropic purposes.” Also, the S.A. Co. has 40,000
acres in South Australia which it bought for
purely philanthropic purposes, taking its solemn
oath to that effect. Bub ib “chucked up ” philan-
thropy long ago, and collared .£1,676,000 of
unearned increment, and is now as hard and
bowelless a concern as ever paid a 15 per cent. div.
Premier Turner’s civil remonstrance re the
S.A.-Maoriland commercial treaty, as being calcu-
lated to hinder Federation—which it certainly is,
seeing that Maoriland stands out of the Federal
scheme —only evoked from Kingston an irrelevant
snarl about the refusal of Victoria to give up the
barren strip of debateable land on the border.
That miserable scrap of dust promises to be, after
all, the worst stumbling-block in the way of the
Commonwealth. Judging from Kingston’s tone,
ib would seem as if the Maoriland treaty was nego-
tiated mainly because of that patch of disputed
territory. Which is vaguely suggestive of the
man who fired a gun at the French President in
order to draw attention to his new invention for
stuffing hair-mattresses.

M‘llwraith has resigned his various offices
in the Queensland Government, bub still remains
a member of Cabinet without portfolio. He will
probably never come back to Queensland, but he
will also probably remain more or less a Minister
without portfolio to the day of his death. Even
Parkes at his best never hung on to the shadow of
office half so tenaciously as M'llwraith.
Premier Reid intimated to Britain that
N.S.W. Governor’s allowances would be for the
future curtailed. In one way this is quite right,
but where was Mr. Reid’s authority for so acting ?
The country which allows a Premier to anticipate
Parliament in one direction cannot justly com-
plain if it finds its Premier anticipating ib, or
misinterpreting it, in another direction. Young
democracies must be ever on the watch to prevent
little fellows, mere bubbles on the political waters,
from arrogating to themselves the right of talking
for the nation in advance.
An enormous amount of the £750,000
owing to the Victorian Treasury as selectors’
arrears is due, not from genuine selectors but
from banks, syndicates, money-lenders and pri-
vate jobbers who have swallowed up the original
holders. The selector himself pays up reasonably
well, but the syndicate and the jobber never pay
at all. Next time Parliament is in recess a good
work would be done by summarily forfeiting
every selection which is seriously in arrears. It
would do little harm to the genuine farmer anyhow,
and it would do the Treasury a lob of good. Bub
ib would have to be done at the beginning of a
long recess ; if Parliament was sitting the
Assembly jobbers would rise as one man and cast
the Government downstairs and rescind the for-
feiture on the spot.
The administration of justice, in its initial
stage, by the ignorant, the brutal, and the preju-
diced is a scandal so enormous that future ages
will wonder how a generation content with such a
clumsy barbarism could have had the audacity to
call itself civilised. Why—when every other
public servant has to show qualification for his
duties—should the chair of preliminary justice be
filled, as often as not, by a leaden-witted lout who
has been pitchforked into it for any or every
reason bub the possession of that intellectual
acumen which is the prime requisite for even the
humblest adjudicatory function ? Nothing bub
the mutton-headed conservatism of the British
race would thus continue to apply to an over-com-
plicated social system the crude device of a time
when laws were as few as the composition of
society was simple. Uninstructed magistracy is
more than mediaeval —ib is archaic !
Bryan O’Loghlen’s defeat at Carlton (Vio.)
by the almost unknown Barrett looks as if the
hairless baronet was finally extinct. The wonder
is that he lasted so long. Poor old O Loghlen at
his best was a finesample of the wild Irish gossoon
when he wears a bell-topper and a boiled shirt,
and tries to look civilised. He would have been
an acquisition to any Irish drama, bub why the
country ever took him seriously, or how he ever
contrived to take himself seriously, are questions
that will never be answered. Bryan was the
Handy Andy of politics, and yet with all his
faults he was worth preserving. He was a fine
sample of what a politician ought not to be, and ib
would have almost been worth while to re-elect
him, so that the public might always have him
before them, and might know, by looking at him,
not to elect any more men of his sorb.
Trenwith’a grievance is the attempt to
form a Liberal Association in Victoria while there
is already the Labor and Land League, which, he
contends, is doing all the work. The old idea
exists that there must be a distinction between
the Liberal and the Radical—the Bob Lowe and
Pearson theory that provision must be made for
the men of culchaw, who sympathise with the
dim masses afar off.

Neither Parkes nor Dibbs spoke on the
second reading of Reidfc Local Government Bill,
though both have been incubating measures of a
similar character for the better part of their lives.
The absence of the pair of chiefs was accidental,
but the fact is still remarkable.
Melb. Argus, reviewing the jusfc-published
life of the late Charles Bradlaugb, spea&s of his
“ brutal and foolish attempt to force his way into
the House of Commons m spite of the rules.”
Bradlaugh’s '‘brutality ” consisted in going to the
House to take his seat in accordance with the law,
and being thrown out and down the steps into
Palace Yard by several big policemen who tore
his coat off and strained every muscle of his body !
It is simply not the fact that he acted in spite of
the rules. He did the very opposite. The pious,
Christian House of Commons broke its own rules
in order to violently eject a wicked Atheist. The
House apologised for it afterwards, too, and ex-
punged the resolutions of expulsion from its
records. But it waited until he was on bis death-
bed to do this, and Charles Bradlaugh never knew
that he had been vindicated by and to the people
he fought for so well.
Dear Bulletin, From a democratic
standpoint there can be no doubt that the princi-
ple of paying M’s.P. for their services as such is
perfectly sound ; but the most casual observer
cannot fail to see that in its application there is
one grave defect. A remedy which suggests itself
to me is this: Render persons chosen by the
people to represent them independent of those in
possession of political power by continuing to re-
munerate them until the Parliament to which
they have been elected shall die of effluxion of
time, or until they resign, or from any cause
become legally incapacitated. lam aware that the
adoption of this course might tend to produce an
over-readiness to oust governments; but even if
it did, wmuld it not still be by far the less of
two undoubted evils ?—Yours, W. Gallagher.
[But M.’sP. don’t cling to their seats solely for
their salaries. They clung just as bard before
salaries were invented. They cling for all the
profits of Parliament—perks, influence, gratifiei
vanity, family kudos (“Pa’s a member now!”)
the library, the club, and a host of other things.—
Ed. B.]

POSTAL BOXES ON CARS,
The Railway Commissioners announce
that the postal boxes fitted on the tram cabs
will be in operation after Sunday next.
All cars running into Bridge-street will
be provided with these boxes, so that any
person may post letters while the trams
are running.

THE GOVERNOR'S SEND-OFF.
lu connection with the enforced half-holiday in
the public service, a question has arisen with re-
gard to the pay of the non-clerical hands in the
Railway Department ;' and, as matters stand, the
working-men of the Department will not thank
the Government for the want of discrimination
shown in declaring a public holiday which was
not asked for. The salaried officers, the highest-
paid members of the service, lose nothing ; but
the daily-paid men, belonging to the classes whose
sufferings during the depression made the late
Governor’s “ heart bleed,” will be losers by the
balf-heliday.—News Item.

The really most noticeable circumstance
in Melbourne s farewell to Lord Hopetoun was its
comparative quietude. The alleged business of
the crowd was to cheer their dear departing lord
on his ’Omeward way, but most of them neglected
their duty so thoroughly that any intelligent
foreigner must have wondered why they troubled
to line the route at all. Three out of every four
of the fatuous sightseers in Collins-street restricted
their enthusiasm to a silent grin when the vice-
regal carriage passed. Lady Hopetoun’s bow was
the one thing that visibly affected the populace.
The unhappy Countess had, no doubt, made up
her mind to do the right thing once before she
retired from office, and this unprecedented bow
was the result. She sat bolt upright as ever, and
worked her head up and down with all the energy
of despair. She didn’t incline her body to the
right or the left. She simply gazed straight
before her with an unflinching shamrock eye, and
beat the air with her chin, 90 times to the minute.
Lady H.’s bow was a last public testimony to the
strange fact that she came out here in a state of
dense ignorance concerning the tricks and manners
of “exalted ” persons. Yet one might have taken it
for granted that Hopetoun’s wife saw the Princess
of Wales, and other old hands at the game, taking
part in some precious demonstration when she
tripped to London a time ago. Apparently the
colleen did not see a royal automaton at work.
Or perhaps—and this is more likely—she can’t
learn how to do it.
• * •
Lord Hopetoun was slightly under the in-
fluence of grovel for several days before he left
Melbourne, and his speeches began to be bom-
bastic. At the Town Hall dinner (where he
graciously promised to accept the first offer of an
Australian Gov.-Generalship) Hopey said : “ I
belong to the same church which my ancestors
belonged to for a good many centuries, and I am
not ashamed to say so.” Now, in the first place,
the Presbyterian Church is only four centuries
old, and, in the second place, H.E. can’t pretend
to know much about his ancestors prior to 1703,
when the family got the first taste of title.
* * *
A rather absurd fuss was made in and out
of the Melbourne press over the departure of Gov.
and Lady Hopetoun. From the wholesale manner
in which the “ departure ” ceremony was boomed
days in advance, it is no wonder that crowds of

his successor’s, but he kept open-house the whole
time to a crowd of English loafers who didn’t care
about the expense of hotel living. And surely, if
Gov. Loch, who had only his salary to live on,
could save a little at the end of his five years, the
stories of Gov. Hopetoun “ blowing ” £lO,OOO a
year above his salary are a trifle over the mark.
• # *
During the visit of a certain vice-regal
pair to an Australian country town, the landlady
of the honored pub. called upon her scowlsome
ladyship the day after the arrival and expressed
the hope that everything had proved satisfactory.
“ If it wasn’t, you’d have heard of it before now,”
was the snappy retort of “ Her Excellency.” Bub
the pub. got even in the long run, for the three
days’ bill of .£BO froze the spinal marrow of the
very “adjacent” Governor.
# » •
Hopetoun thanked the Melbourne police
before departing I have always received the
prompt assistance of all the members of the force
when I required it,” he wrote. Yes, and (this is
only a goak) the humble taxpayer has so often to
content himself with a lamp-post.
By the way, his lordship even called upon the
humble tradesman who half-soled his noble boots,
to say adieu, and express his satisfaction with the
country in general on parting.
# * #
The indecent dishonesty of a “loyal”
mob surpasses all comparison. The well-dressed
rabble who “rushed” Spencer-street station to
witness the departure of Gov. and Lady Hope-
toun barely waited till the train had steamed
away from the platform, , before they made a
delirious looting raid upon the floral decorations,
rushes, greenery, and choice pot plants, with
which the ugly place had been temporarily beauti-
fied. The women, who, a few minutes before,
had been posing in their politest society manners
before the Gov. and his wife, now bustled,
hunted and jostled each other in a mad panic of
purloining. Grovelling sycophant and greedy
snider are convertible terms.
m * •
Ex-Premier Jeames Patterson, at theHope-
toun farewell banquet: “ One of the most beauti-
ful features of their sojourn here was the manner
in which the earl and countess had engrafted
themselves on our society.” But the manner in
which “ our society ” struggled to engraft itself on
the earl and countess was much more notable.
• # •
Very significantly, the Argus leader on
Hopetoun’s Governorship and departure con-
tained not the slightest reference to the Countess.
The heroic attempt to make the poor lady popular
towards the end was a very sickly failure. Also
significant was the conspicuous absence of Lady
Clarke and Swilliam from the departure cere-
monials.

An old lady died in
Castlemaine (Vic.) the
other day. Her obituary
notice states proudly that
“ deceased was well-con-
nected in the old coun-
try, being a relation of
the late Mayor of Tor-
quay.” A provincial
Mayor in England is
nearly, but not quite as
much thought of as a
policeman in an Austra-
lian country township.
• •
Before the present
Vic. Baby-farming Act
came in force, several
houses, almost within a
stone-throw of Melb.
Lying-in Hospital, did
a roaring business. The
nurses found out the
“good mark” who would
pay, say, .£lO or .£2O to
anyone taking the baby
off her hands, and the
“ shame ” was then ad-
vertised and sold again
for half the amount. The
baby-farmer, if up to
business, never lost sight
of the mother, and black-
mailed her regularly. It
paid a lot better than
poultry-farming.
* #
Barter is still yery
much alive in Australian
bush townships. Shop-
keepers billet their hands
in hotels or boarding-
houses, and the proprie-
tors cut out the board-
money in groceries or
clothes. Vegetables are
exchange 1 for bread,
meat for furniture, and
so on. The local news-
paper’s employes are
each allotted an “adver-
tising ” store, where an
account is run up, and, at
the end of the month or
quarter, the newspaper-
man is debited by the
office with the amount of
his account, and the
storekeeper is credited
with so much off his ad-
vertising score. It’s
awful for the editor when
he feels thirsty several
miles away from his par-
ticular hotel.
» •
The crowd of women who went prepared
to gush over the Hopetoun children, on Govt.
House reception-day, were disappointed. The
two youngsters had been quietly sent away with
the nurse on the previous Saturday by the mail-
boat. Lady Hopetoun preserved an unmoved face
before the visitors’ protestations of disappoint-
ment.
• • •
“ She never bowed so much all the time
she was here,” was the general comment on Lady
Hopetoun’s clockwork sort of acknowledgment of
the crowd’s farewell greetings. But the secret of
such surprising graciousness was a spring cushion ;
and her shy ladyship could no more help her bend-
ing than a doll pulled by a string can help opening
and shutting its eyes. Hopetoun is an artful dog.
NOT DUE YET ,
“ Have you washed this morning, sonny ? ”
Small Boy: “ Naw, only jist had my breakfast !”
Every private house in College-street,
Sydney, is now occupied by one or more
medicoes. The unpretentious residence of the
late Lieut.-Governor Stephen has fallen to
medico. St. Mary’s Cathedral stands at
one end of the street, and a druggist’s at the
other, so that it needs but the addition of an
undertaker’s and the conversion of Cook Park
into a cemetery to make College-street a really
well appointed booking-office for the Hereafter.
• • #
As stated, the late Governor Duff s funeral
will cost N.S.W. Treasury about .£4OOO. In the
U.S. even senators, if they die in office, are buried
at public expense. They are embalmed at a cost
of £l5 or £2l, and casketed and coffined at an
expense of about £7O. Kid gloves are distributed
in quantities, attendants are paid per day, car-
riages are provided for all who like to ride, and if
the senator’s body is to be taken to his home, miles
away from Washington, special Pullman cars are
set apart for the cortege, at an expense sometimes
up to £5OO. Meals, lunches and liquors are pro-
vided for all mourners, and even the church ser-
vices at the grave or vault are paid for out of the
public funds.
“After my three years’ training in the navy
during the fifties, I look upon the local gaols as
first-class boarding-houses.” Thus one Of the re-
leased N.S. W. bank-directors. “ After the luxury
of a seat inParliament, gaol is an aggregation of all
the horrors imaginable.” Thus another, which
shows that the system depends for its success upon
the early training of the subject.
* ( •
One of the most pathetic examples of
civilisation’s tortures was exhibited at the theatre
the other night, when the hero of a certain
somewhat smellful banking affair sat in the
dress • circle. The play was peppered, with
allusion’s to rocky banks and cock-eyed direc-
tors, and so on, and at every such allusion
every head in the circle would turn in the direc-
tion of the fallen magnate, while the gallery
would wave like a wind-blown cornfield, and its
vanguard would hang on by its toes to get a
glimpse of the onoe-great man’s face.

a question in compound proportion for home-
work, one evening, which happened to include the
circumstance of “ men working 10 hours a day in
order to complete a certain work.” Next morning
the unsuspecting teacher, in looking over his little
pack of exercises, found “Jim’s ” sum unattempted
and the following letter enclosed in the page :
Sir,—l refuse to let Jim do his sum you give him last
night has it looks to me to be a slur at 8-hour Bistum
enny sum not more than 8 hours he is welcume to do but
not more.—Yours truely, Abram Blank, Senr.

The Melbourne Women’s Suffrage Socie-
ties—there are three of them—propose to agitato
for a law raising the age of consent to 18. If they
succeed, the age of request should be raised to 21.
What is sauce for the goose, &c. The only answer
to which is that as they say in “ Rebellious
Susan,” there never was any sauce for the gander,
“or our grandmothers would have discovered it.”

• • •
A Yic. penwoman, who wears her hair
short, relates that on a late visit to Sydney she
travelled in the same train—but not in the same
carriage—as a bride and bridegroom. She and
another girl having, however, a carriage to them-
selves, lay down on the seats and covered them-
selves with rugs. At a wayside station the people
had been told by the train-officials that there were
on board the train a bride and bridegroom, and
some intrepid loafers who walked by the car-
riages, peered into the windows. When they
saw the short-haired girl and the other one lying
on the seats, they took them for the bride and
bridegroom aforesaid, and began to “barrack.”
“ And would you believe it,” says the penwoman,
“ I had to stand up, throw off my rug, and show
them my skirts before they would go away.”
They had taken her for the bridegroom 1 If any-
one had taken her for a bride she possibly wouldn’t
have been so annoyed.
• # #
A metropolitan newspaper says on the sub-
ject of reducing the N.S.W. Governor’s salary :
“To say that there is no need for the discharge
of expensive functions is to say that the ideas and
the traditions and the observances of our social
system must be revolutionised.” .Seeing that after
most Government House “functions” the names
of the editor of that journal, and of the ladies
of his family and of his “proprietors” are all
printed in his own columns, this barefaced high-
society cry for sandwiches at the expense of Hodge
is exhilarating.
• • •
The proposal to shut up Sydney Zoo.,
based on remarks of the frightened president of
that institution, registers about the lowest notch
of the public’s conception of Governmental duty.
Modern ideas make the Government the instruc-
tor-in-chief of the nation, and what secular
instruction can educate so effectually in one im-
portant direction as the Zoo. ? So far from
shutting up the place because of insufficient out-
side support, the council of management should
apply to Parliament to have the institution placed
on the same grade as the primary schools. If pub
to a vote to-morrow, The Bulletin ventures to
say that Parliament would take this view, and
there can be no doubt of the advantage of such a
resolution.

IN THE STREET.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Glare of sun in crowded* street,
Weary tread of tired feet;
Wolfish gleam in every eye,
Grasping greed is passing by.
Branded deep in every line,
Lust of power, pride of place,
Stamping out the hope divine
Nature prints on human face.
Woman —fickle, shallow, vain;
Man—full-bellied, gross, and big ;
Offspring—well, expect the same :
Human pea-hen ! human pig !
Ruling with the catching phrase
Echoed oft by servile press,
Tried and wanting—with amaze
People grasp its emptiness.
Tell me, where then is the wrong
Trampling down the toiling crowd ?
Work is thrust upon the strong,
Power is given to the loud.
MAURICE F. KEMP.

cremation on the sea-shore, and, therefore, the
need for a properly fitted-up crematorium becomes
all the more urgent. There is, undoubtedly, a
growing demand for cremation, but the dead
must be combusted decently and in order, and
picnic parties must not come and boil the billy
alongside the funeral pyre. Also, public opinion
is averse to the small boys of the neighborhood
being allowed to skirmish round afterwards among
the ashes in search of bones, and it holds that the
solemnity of the occasion is marred by casual
onlookers making remarks and chewing tobacco
while the operations are in progress. Cremation
on the sea-shore is altogether too suggestive of the
old British festivity of roasting a bullock whole.
# • •
F. : “ The press criticisms on the Melb.
cremation fiasco make me very tired. Every
week the bodies of a dozen decent people are
given over to maggots and nobody winces. My
splendid body shall not, provided I leave a few
dollars behind, suffer such living death. A
cremation fiasco is preferable to corruption, as
long as a clean state of ashes is reached at last.
This body of mine has been the means of most of
my pleasures and the cause of none of my troubles,
and I love it too much to share it among loath-
some crawlers. If it were attainable I might
prefer artistic embalming so that this body might
remain beautiful for ever. But, in the alternative,
I prescribe myself a poet’s obsequies, Ac dawn
my body shall be taken in a chariot with silent
tires drawn by four blood horses full speed to the
crematory. The resultant ashes shall be taken to
a place where bush and ocean meet, there to be
cast upon the wind. My dear body must have a
clean exit.”
• * *
A few years ago a very handsome and
most-talked-of Society woman disappeared from
Australia in a final cloud of scandal, in which
a scion of nobility got somehow mixed.
Her whereabouts has since been a mystery,
which is now cleared up. A man, erstwhile one of
the most intimate of her set, who has just returned
from Paris, met her there. It was at a convivial
supper, and she was “ablaze of diamonds”—a
recognised leader of that world which at one time
would not have been mentioned by men in her
presence. Her former companion went up to
greet her, but she merely stared him in the face
and moved away. The whirligig of time !

• # #
In the 200 and odd islands comprising the
Fiji group you could not find a single rabbit It
would be a bad day for the Crown colony that
produces bananas all the year round if these pests
got on to the plantations.
• # »
No “native” (no matter if he be Indian,
foreign labor, or Fijian) can in Fiji procure in-
toxicating liquor without a permit; and only one
or two chiefs have the necessary permits. Once,
a highly-educated Maori, dressed a la tourist,
joined a party of trippers in a “ bob-in ” at Suva,
but when it came to serving the foreigner of dark
appearance, the barmaid stammeringly asked him
(as is her legal right) for his permit. Of course,
he never heard of such a thing, and, because he
had to be content with ginger-ale, he went out,
threatening to punch Thurston’s head for classing
a Maori chief’s son with a common native and
dirty coolie. In fact, the same awkward thing
happened, it is said, to a very high and wealthy
Indian nabob on tour, who called for a bottle of
champagne.
• • *
A Maoriland country parson combines
dentistry with praying, and doe? a rattling good
business at both. The other day, at a squatter’s
tea-table, he remarked, “ I drew 17 teeth to-day
from a gentleman without gas.” “One of the
storekeepers, Mr. ?” “Oh, no, from a
Chinaman.” The squatter shuddered. For had
he not had an old stump neatly extracted himself
with the same instruments !

“ Ay, a’ ae 00. ” Which, being interpreted, signi-

• • •
Max O’JRell has been saying in London
that he hopes to see the day when the English
workman will drink the light wines of Australia
instead of beer and spirits. Considering that the
Australian workman, who is on the spot, never
drinks-wine, if he can help it, but always beer,
when he can get it, M. Blouet is likely to have to
wait a long time for his hope to he realised. Try
to imagine how, for instance, a British bricklayer
would look who, after a hard spell of work, was
offered a glass of chablis instead of a pot of beer !

Somebody advertised in the Sydney Chinese
paper that he had land to lease on the Botany
tram-line, suitable for poultry-farms, applicants
to write to Box So-and-so, G.P.O. This is a
literal translation of what appeared : “ Land to
be had by yearly payments near Botany fire-
wheel ; extremely excellent for nourishing fowls
and ducks. Write your requests to Box—, Big
Letter House, Sydney.”

A hot discussion on the duty of celebrating
St. Patrick’s Day on 17th March (Sunday) instead
of on 18th was brought to a sudden stop in a
Sydney committee - room by the apparently ir-
relevant interjection of a member—“ Pubs, are shut
on Sunday.” That settled the matter.

the reporter lately wrote;
“Dr. Johnson felt the
deceased’s pulse before
prescribing.” The printer
set it up : “ Dr. Johnson
felt deceased’s purse be-
fore prescribing.”

In reality, there was only one figure at a
recent imposing Southern funeral —the undertaker.
He was good when he composed his features to
the expression of decorous grief which the occasion
demanded ; he was good when his face shone with
pride at the thought that he—HE—was “plant-
ing ” one of the most distinguished persons ever
undertaken in his province; but when his grief
and his pride mingled, when half his countenance
was in glaring sunlight and the other half in
deepest shadow, whan his nose was awry with
gloom and his mouth bubbled with exultant
jollity, then—ah, then he was irresistible.

IT IS EASY TO ASK QUESTIONS.
A child can ask questions that a wise man can’t
answer. Yes; ami there are some questions that
the ablest doctors don't like to have people ask
them. Say a question of this sort —while we are
about it, however, we might as well tell the story
straight away and have done with it.
Mrs. Sarah Mace was very ill with influenza.
That was in February, 1892, the time of the
epidemic. The attack was severe. She was
suddenly seized with a violent pain in the head
(both front and back) and temples. She had pain
in all her joints, too, and was hot and feverish.
She went to bed and sent for the doctor. He came,
examined her, took her temperature —over 100
degrees—and said very little, like a wise man.
The lady could not turn herself in bed. She lived
on slops, such as milk and broth; she could not
swallow a morsel of solid food. Her husband
pressed her to take strong food, saying, “If you
don’t eat you will starve.” Right enough ; but he
forgot that when one can’t eat he can’t eat, and
there’s an end.
Well, she got weaker and weaker, and fell away
until there wasn’t much left of her but skin and
bone. Then, in her anxiety, she put her question :
“ Doctor, do you think I shall get well?"
The doctor’s answer was true and honest; we
like and respect him for it. He said, “Mrs. Mace,
Ido not know.” He couldn’t tell. Neither could
any other doctor. No ; not even if he had a string
of titles to his name as long as a kite’s tail. But
did she get well ? Wait a bit. We must hark
back a minute now.
Along about the 10th of March, 1880, Mrs. Mace
began to feel tired, languid and weary, as if her
work was too much for her, she said. She had a
bad taste in the mouth, poor appetite, and what-
ever she ate gave her pain at the chest and heart.
‘‘l had,” she continues, “a dull heavy pain at the
right side and between the shoulders, and a sinking
feeling at the pit of the stomach, and a rising of
foul gas in my throat. Later on I had rheumatic
pains in my heart, chest and back. I sent for a
doctor, who attended me for six weeks, but I got
worse. One day the cook said, * Why don’t jrou
try Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup ? ’ —this being
a medicine my mistress (I was living with a lady
in Camden Town) kept in the house for family use.
I did so, and was soon as strong as ever.
Years fied away, and in 1891 she had the first
attack of influenza, and in 1892, the second, al-
ready described in part. We now complete the
account of the 1892 attack.
Mrs. Mace says: “The doctor continued to
attend me for five weeks, and I took his medicines
for that time, but gained no strength. After hav-
ing suffered for seven weeks I said to my husband,
' I will now see what Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup will do for me. Perhaps it may cure
influenza as well as other ailments.’ I carried out
this resolution ; began taking the Syrup, and was
soon on my feet again, and have since kept in
excellent health, taking an occasional dose when
needed. (Signed) Sabah Mace, Monk’s Farm,
Great Warley, Brentwood, Essex, August 27th,
1892.”
Now we have never advertised Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup as a remedy for influenza. Yet
the facts stated by Mrs. Mace cannot be disputed.
What is the conclusion ? A very simple one in-
deed. She did not have influenza until her system
had been debilitated by indigestion and dyspepsia.
It is such people generally (almost wholly) that
all epidemic diseases attack. The dreaded cholera
scarcely ever touches anybody except a dyspeptic
—cholera is a malady of the bowels only. The
more we hear of disease the more the proof piles
up, showing that persons with a sound digestion
are safe against dying of anything except violence
or old age. The gyrupcured Mrs. Mace’s influenza
by taking away the ground it stood on—the tor-
pidity of her digestion.
She once asked, “ Doctor, do you think I shall
get well?” Time and Mother Seigel have said,
“Yes.”
At Poverty Point .
Dainty Deoima Moore, of the Gaiety Girlhood, comes to
Australia with the reputation of being one of the most
delightful singers and dancers on the English comic
opera stage. She is the tenth daughter of an analytical
chemist at Brighton (Gag.), and has three or four other
sisters in the front rank of the profesh. Miss Moore, who
studied under Gu rini and Carrodus, was oast as Oasilda
in the first production of “The Gondoliers,” and since
then has steadily gone ahead. She is a clever violinist
and a piquant actress, with a sweet and flexible voice and
great charm of manner. Her sister, Eva, married H. V.
Esmond, the original Cayley Drummle of “ The Second
Mrs. Tanqueray,” and Eddie Remon of “The Mas-
queraders.” Decima herself is said to be either married
or engaged to be married to a gentleman belonging to the
visiting co.
The sprightly Lottie Venne, the glorious Hayden Coffin,
and the pert Parisian, Juliette Nesville, are the only mem-
bers of the original Gaiety Girl Co. absent from the Aus-
tralian contingent.
Gaiety Girl Grace Paiotta is one of the youngest in ex-
perience of the visiting bevy of beauteous maidens. Her
only stage engagement previous to “The Gaiety Girl”
was in " Madame Angot ”at the end of ’93. She heard
some girls talking in a London ’bus about the trial of
voices at the “Cri.” She went, was heard, and was
engaged. She was horn in Vienna, but has little accent.
Her charming mezzo-soprano was trained by Randegger.
She is an intellectual maiden, with leanings towards the
legitimate drama, and fences well—which is news for
the stage-door dudes.
“ Trilby,” the play on the feet, will be seen in Aus-
tralia this year. Meanwhile, Miss Rathbone’s superb feet
are displayed, in a half-life size photo., at Falk’s Oollins-
street window. They are lovely trotters, beautifully
normal, utterly devoid of nubbly corns and the twists,
screws, and turns of fashionable boots.
A young Melb. actress has her photos, scattered about,
signed with her stage-name, but she used to sign them
with her real, name, under which she first performed. It
is a mistake to take aflotitious one, unless you are labelled
with a horror like Joshua Bugg, which the owner
changed to Norfolk Howard, so giving a new designa-
tion to a clas,s of inseotivora. See what an advantage it
is for a performer to have a name like Hayden Coffin.
How mistaken he would have been to alter it to Choi-
mondeley, instead of wreathing garlands around the, skull.
Maggie Moore, who is liberal with her cash, required,
prior to leaving Melbourne for Sydney, a big box, and on
enquiry at a shop, was asked £2 10s. for the article
required. Maggie stared with surprise, but the shopman
would not lower the figure ; so she left, and later on sent
her cabman to again price the article. The shopman,
unaware that'cabby had come from the theatre, after a
few words, let him have the trunk for 265.!
Two managers of juvenile minstrel troupes that play in
Melbourne and suburbs to “silver coin” audiences, are
sail to clear £2O each weekly after paying exs. Some of
the performers—girls and boys of 13 and 15—receive £2
10s. weekly. Grown-up members of several dramatic
companies playing to poor houses round the Melbourne
suburbs, fathers and mothers of families, who give really
good performances, have to'be content with a mere pit-
tance, sometimes amounting to 15s. or 18s. weekly.

A great mistake in choosing the models of Living Pic-
tures is made in picking big girls. The big girl is shapely
enough when properly “ fixed up,” but she seldom or
never is made as well as her diminutive sister. It is the
Patfie Browns, the Vi Varleys, the Florence Harriotts, and
the Katie Rarrys who are tile real Venuses of the stage ;
for every large one like Sophie Harris there are a dozen
little ’uns of almost perfect symmetry. It is the same
with men. Take the boxers, for instance. Corbetts and
Jackson are each poorly-muscled in the legs; Slavin is
too thin in the waist—and so on right through the heavy-
weight division. Among the nine and ten-stoner-*, how-
ever—Jack M’Gowan, Jim Anthony, Tim Hegarty, ami
the rest—nearly every one eiiters the ring like a clear-cut
Apollo. Look at little Griffo !
Sophie Harris hounds into London popularity as Selim,
in the Crystal Palace panto. She acted here only for fun,
her people having been wealthy before the boom.
Will Birnes, a rising Melb. artist, has just sent to
Augustus Harris, London, a set of designs for an Austra-
lian ballet. It consists of 25 paintings—each one a hallet-
girl garbed to represent some brilliant Australian bird.
He is the first to think of such a thing. Not only the
exact coloring, hut the character of each bird is capitally
hit off. The scheme is original and dazzling, and Mr. and
Mrs. Dacre (Miss Amy Roselle) were greatly struck.
When Madame Bernhardt was here she slid that Mr.
Barnes was one of the most original designers she had
met, and so said Mrs. Potter.

entitled, “If Shakespeare came to London?” VVelij and
if he did, he would inevitably be collared by the Smvthes,
father and son, for an Australian lecturing-tour. That’s
all about it.
