Louise Jordan Miln’s book of travel, “ When we were
Strolling Players in the East,” is already in its 2nd
edition. Its chief illustration depicts the authoress as
Desdemona—which recalls a certain fireman’s benefit at
Melb. Opera House. The play was “ Othello,” with Miln
himself as the Moor, and Louise J. as Desdemona. In the
bedchamber scene, Miln, as always, was lugging Louise
round the room by her back-hair, when one fireman in
the pit asked his friend, “What’s the nigger trailin' her
round like that for, Bill?” To which the explanation
came ; “ Why, you fool, can’t you see the (carnation)
woman went to bed with her boots on 1 ” Truly enough,
the satin slippers of the previous scenes were plainly
visible beyond the hem of her nightdress.
Stokes Craven, who wrote “ An Innocent Abroad ” for
the opening of Edward Terry’s London season, toured for
two years in Australia with poor Phil Day. While here
he wrote “Mixed,” “ Bad Lads,” and “Hide and Seek,”
all three-act farces, for Phil.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
N.S.W. League of Wheelmen’s meeting drew a
big crowd to Sydney Cricket Ground on Saturday. The
afternoon was fine and warm, and the grass track in good
order ; but a strong and gusty wind put record-beating
out of the question. The four events drew 117 competi-
tors—every province but Westralia being represented;
and that interesting hybrid, the cash amateur, had, on the
whole, a very good day out. Top money was offered in
the Sydney Wheel Race, about two miles, with £lOO, £l5,
and £5, for Ist, 2nd, and 3rd man ; whde the League Cup,
half-mile, gave a first prize of £2O, the N.S.W. Half-mile
Championship was worth £l6 and the League’s gold
medals, and there was a 1 mile Maiden Handicap to teaoh
the young idea how to spin.
The wheelmen were above the average lot. Con-
spicuous was Wyburd, the Bathurst footballer—a hairy,
brawny athlete in a black silk Jacket decked with skull
and cross-bones. Davis, the South Australian, insisted on
riding in professional tog ; and, had he won a r ice, would
have been protested against on that account. Probably
the protest would have succeeded, for the cash amateur
conscience is remarkably tender on points like this. Neil
Matterson, ex-sculler, in shining red and white, was the
tallest cyclist engaged.
In the first event, the first heat of the League
Cup, Osborne fell, destroying his own chance and that of
Lewis, who had to run wide outside the track where the
deep turf made heavy going. The heat was won by
Weaver, a much-fancied Sydney man who, favored with a
40yds. handicap, just beat Middleton, the Victorian crack.
Middleton is a Victorian miner ; he struggles terribly
when racing, working and wriggling from the crown of
the head to the sole of his foot, and trusting more to
power than skill. But a gamer man never entered a con-
test. In the second heat, Neil Matterson and Chris.
Neilsen, the two ex-scullers, both succumbed to Everitt.
Third heat was won by a maiden racer, A. J. Smith, with
the dark-horse, Payne, second. Fourth heat fell to Rath-
gen ; and fifth to' Meadham, the ex-amateur swimmer.
Sixth heat was collared by Cathie, the Victorian; Megson,
of N.S.W., finishing second with a splendid rush. The
final will be run next Saturday.
Tiie three heats and final of the One Mile
Maiden Handicap can he dismissed in a sentence, for the
race was a gift to the limit man, A. J. Smith.
For the first heat of the Half Mile Champion-
ship representatives had entered from N.S.W., Victoria,
Queensland, Tasmania, and Maoriland. Simpson, of
N.S.W., was one ; he had not had a real race since his
accident nearly 12 months ago, when he fell in the ten-
mile race with Kerr. The heat was cleverly won by
Lewis, with Simpson second, and Boyd, of Maoriland,
third. Middleton, of Vio., won the second heat, with
Megson, the N.S.W. champion, four yards behind. Mid-
dleton was plainly not husbanding his strength in the
least; he had determined to win everything. The final
was postponed till after the heats of the great race of the
day, the Sydney Wheel Race.
Bitterly were these heats contested. Neil Mat-
terson won the first, with Morton, of Vic., second. The
second was splendidly taken by Wyburd; Cathie, of Vic.,
2. Megson, who was on scratch with Davis, of S.A., gave
up after doing half the distance. The scratch men had
been handicapped out of these heats, and the game
Middleton came out for the third heat to prove it. There
was a man in front of him, Payne, with a handicap
of 150yds. The pace was fast, Middleton struggling
desperately to catch the leaders ; while all the time Payne
kept in a good place in the ruck until the critical moment
in the final lap, when he shot out like an arrow and won
by 10yds. from Middleton, who, turning his machine upon
the grass, fell of! exhausted and had to be carried in.
That terrible race did for Middleton; neverthe-
less he came out for the Half-Mile ‘Championship to race
against Megson, who had wisely drawn out of his heat in
the Wheel Race, and was now as . fresh as a lark. He
romped in, with Boyd, of Maoriland, second, and Lewis
third.
Middleton couldn’t face the final of the Wheel
Race, which was merely a repetition of Payne’s heat.
For two or three laps Cathie and Morton, the Vic. limit
men, led together, pursued by 12 others, who hung
100 yds. behind in a hunch. Then the big form of Neil
Matterson overhauled the leaders; and in the last lap
Cathie, the Victorian, was leading by 50.yds., and Matter-
son promised to come second. But, 350yds. from home,
Payne shot out again like a flish, and passed the tired
Cathie within a few yards of the post. All was over.
The League managers are delighted : they took
£6BO for the first day, and have next Saturday’s harvest
in prospect; while the two days’ expenses are only about
£6OO.
* * * *
Mr. J. A. Scarr, for nearly 40 years N.S.W.
Parliamentary shorthand-writer, and for many years
handicapper to the A.J.C. and other clubs, died at Manly
Beach on Sunday last, and was on Monday followed to
the grave by a numerous assemblage of sportsmen and
members of the general public. Mr. Scarr was born at
Campbelltown in 1830, and displayed marked ability in
everything he took up. He was one of the rare men who
could touch pitch without being defiled. He preserved
an unsullied reputation throughout all his long connec-
tion with the turf, and went to his grave mourned as one
of the most kindly and chivalrous of gentlemen.

an investment of four ss. tickets in a Tattersall’s sweep.
The lady’s husband (who bought the tickets at his wife’s
request and w’ith her money) being in difficulties, the
Official Assignee sought to annex the little pile, but failed.
* * ♦ *
Here is a startling possibility if the potentiali-
ties of turf-betting were unlimited. A punter going to
Flemington for Hopetoun farewell meeting, starting with
a pound, backing every winner, and “ playing up the
stuff” every race, would have come out thus:
odds. £.
1— Hope Stakes ..... sto 1 .. 6
2 Niddry Hurdles .. .. 6to 1 .. 42
3 Hopetoun Cup .. .. 10 to 1 .. 462
4 Holywell Steeple .. 10 to 1 .. 5082
5 Ormiston Handicap .. 10 to 1 .. 65,902
6 Bracelet Stakes .. .. 10 to I ~ 614,922
Supposing a seventh race at 10 to 1 also, he would finish
with £6,764,142. It would be easier to back seven winners
than to mark 10 in a Chinese lottery. The weak spot is
not in that, but in the fact that the market is only in-
fluenced by the amount of money invested, and not by
the merits of the spec. Form and performance are
nowhere in it.

• » » *
Be the Hopetoun farewell meeting at Fleming-
ton, it was amusing to read in the Argus that “enjoy-
ment of the afternoon was marred at the close by the fact
that two good horses had to be shot on the course.” The
general public gives no heed to these little matters. At
the end of a “had day,” the enjoyment of a stone-broke
punter would not be marred by the news that all the
bookmakers present had broken their legs and been
slaughtered. * , *
Dear Bulletin, —Please allow me to con-
tradict the recent statement that Sep. Miller never
backs his horses. Every horse he starts, whether
fancied or not, carries £2O stable commission. It
his chance is really good, of course he is backed for
more. But £2O is the minimum. And it pays, too. And
now, Just here is the longlooked-for betting-system. For,
if it pays Mr. Hiller to hack thus every time, why shouldn’t
the casual punter do the same, putting, say, a pound on
each time ?—Walter S.

“Sainmy ” Woods, late of Sydney, who played
for Australia in one of the elevens visiting England, cap-
tained the English football-team in the recent match
against Wales.
• • ♦ *
According to a southern paper, the Y.R.C.
committee will support the Vic. Totalisator Bill. But it
won’t be in the interests of the lamb—it is now a question
of£s. d. with the V.R.O. As The Bulletin said years
ago, the Victorian racing-fever was only a boom—when
men like White, Wallace, O’Loughlin, and others fell out
the bubble would burst. That time has now come. The
papers, bookies, and trainers now “live on the game”
direct. The papers make the favourites, the bookies own
them, and the lamb backs them. The papers exist, the
Chosen drink gold-top and drive carriages, and the lamb
walks home without the price of a long beer.
♦ * * ♦
The V.R.C. is ripe for revolution. The great
hamper is that awkward provision which stops the club
from charging to the Flat, but the course is fenced in so
strictly that, whenever it gets the power, it can charge at
once. Nevertheless, the forward section of the Club
wants a start to be made with monthly meetings, or
something like that.

That clever jockey, “ Jemmy ” Hayes, has gone
to ’Frisco. He certainly had his faults, but, as he was
only a boy, his punishment is generally considered over-
severe.
* ♦ ♦ »
The English cricketers left Spencer - street
station (Melb.) as little noticed as if they had been a gang
of unemployed on their way to Kooweerup Swamp. Lord
Hopetoun happened to be leaving by the same train and
the crowd hadn’t a cheer or a hiccup to spare for the
others. People can get drunk on only one kind of emo-
tion at a time. And, anyhow, they would have been dis-
posed to regard the departure of the cricketers with the
cold dry eye of sobriety. Australia thinks a good deal
less of Stoddart’s men since they won the final test-match.
If we had beaten them, we should have liked to keep them
and console them : hut, as they heat us, the sooner they
hurry out of sight the better.
» ♦ * ♦
It looks as if the English cricketers had made
a special effort to end their Australian tour in ablaze
of glory. The Adelaide wicket has been generally sup-
posed unfavorable to them ; but in the final match with
S.A. they managed to put up the record score of 609,
Ward making 219, Ford 106, and Brown 101. This was
without the help of Stoddart, who is still unwell. The
South Australians did respectably enough, making 397 in
their first innings ; but, at time of writing, it looked as if
they would hardly aveit a one-innings defeat.
♦ ♦ ♦ •
J. L. Sullivan isn’t dead, after all. But, for all
practical purposes, he might just as well be dead. As
Hanian was the first of the modern scullers, so Sullivan
led the latter-day glove-fighters. Sullivan was a cham-
pion in his time, and about the first exponent of the
potency of the swinging right on the point of the jaw,
hut it must be confessed that Kilrain, Ryan, Mitchell,
Burke, Smith and the men of his day were poor cattle
indeed whereon to build up such a world-wide reputation
as Sullivan enjoyed. Jack Dempsey Was looked upon in
America as simply invincible in his class—barring a
chance knock-out by La Blanche—until Australia came
along, and then “the band played Annie Rooney.” And
so it mi'iht have been with Sullivan, hut it must be con.
fessed of him that he beat all opponents until he became
a h°er-sodden and heart-diseased man. When Sullivan’s
spirit does depart it can lay to itself the unction that
whilst amongst us its owner occupied more space in th’
world’s press than any ten men the Universe has yet pro-
duced, bar none.
* * » *
Trenton, champion sire, handsomest horse at
the stud, and second prize in St. Alban's lottery, is 14
years old. How time flies ! It seems but yesterday that
he ran second and third for two Melb. Cups—beaten both
times by weight.

Quong Tart has been appointed starter for one of
Sydney Cycling League’s races, hut, in fairness to competi-
tors, he should take a lesson from an expert like Frank
Hewitt. Ere touching the trigger Quong grimaces and
gives the game away.
♦ * ♦ »
The brothers Ballinger, who fired off for Maori-
land shooting championship this year, live in Wellington.
Both were ex-champions, and one of them, a few years
ago, only had to hit the target in his very last shot, after
five days’ firing, to win the belt. Being young and flurried,
he missed.

The celebrated greyhound, Fullerton, has proved
worthless for stud purposes. Experienced breeders usually
prefer the performer’s brother to the performer himself,
whose constitution and vigor seem to be affected by
training. The same rule is not observed in the turf
world, though Ormonde, despite his high price, was a
failure. The Australian Navigator, a great racehorse,
died the other day without leaving anything of note,
though he had fair chances.

Australasia, 257 George-street, Sydney. Note. —
These remedies have cured where operation and
all other means have failed.*

Mr. D. Bernard, the well-known Sydney
art importer, has opened new premises at 430
George-street as a commodious gallery and work-
rooms. Mayor Lees presided at the formal cere-
mony which marked the occasion, and many
Sydney citizens attended and inspected Mr.
Bernard’s fine collection of pictures, engravings,
and objects of art and vertu. It is Mr. Ber-
nard’s intention to conduct his business on the
most modern lines, and he announces that
students and visitors will always be welcome to his
attractive emporium.

The approach of winter has evidently pro-
duced much activity in the creative minds of
fashionable artistes. Young ladies’ evening cos-
tumes are going to be very pleasant to the eye, at
least if one may take those seen at Messrs. Hichs,
Atkinson and Sons’establishment in Collins-street,
as a criterion. This firm, however, it seems, has
opened new dressmaking rooms specially to cater
for the younger generation, and the combination
of interestingly low prices, elegant taste and high-
class finish will be appreciated by the youth of
social Melbourne. *
Paikes’ appearance as president of the
little crowd (including the inevitable Knapp and a
lot of parsons) who attended at Sydney Temper-
ance Hall “ to protest against further delay in
Local Option legislation ” was a most piteous cir-
cumstance. The veteran trimmer is now living
on mice. “ I have great pleasure in taking the
chair,” he said, “ though I am not here as a total
abstainer.” It recalls what he used to say of yore
at Orange celebrations : “ I have great pleasure,
&0., but I am not nor have I ever been an Orange*
man ’’-—and then in your mind’s eye you could see
him put his fingers to his nose and softly wink.
“I do not identify myself with the Local Option
movement as an opponent or as a person
advocating any unneighborly feeling against the
persons engaged in the liquor-traffic (derisive
laughter). What has that idiot to laugh at?”
Well, that idiot was probably laughing at the
idea of the ancient and plausible deceiver who had
played see-saw until he wore out the middle of the
plank, and w r ho never felt so comfortable or
appeared to such good advantage as when he had
two bottles of sparkling Burgundy and a roast
duck under his belt, having the cast-iron im-
pudence to stand up in public and snuffle that
“they knew that the consumption of intoxicating
drinks was not necessary to the health and real
enjoyment of any human being under the sun.
(Applause.)” Booh!

At Sydney Centennial Park, in exchange
for half-a-day’s labor, men receive rations
sufficient to last a week. During the first
nine days 971 men went to work, and the
week before last no less than 1334 men were en-
gaged. Up to Tuesday fortnight the number work-
ing daily was from 150 to 445, and 15 per cent, of
these were single men.

novel invention,” from amongst the many offered for approval the Company invites subscriptions

A case of extra-uterine gestation is re-
ported by Dr. Bakins, of Eohuca (Vic.), in the
Marcli Medical Gazette. A living, healthy
child was delivered by opening the abdomen, and
the mother was well on the way to recovery when,
out of morbid curiosity, she removed the antisep-
tic dressing and died 17 days after the operation.
A Westralian police-magistrate was sitting
on his verandah one Sunday morning recently,
wondering whether it was time to get ready for
church, when a man came running up the road,
dashed in the gate, and breathlessly appealed to
the P.M. for protection from an enemy who wished
to assault him. The magistrate hadn’t time to
collect his thoughts ere the pursuer came panting
up and made straight for the first-comer, who
ran through the open door of the house into the
parlor, and tried to hide under the sofa. He was
dragged out, however, before he got half-way
under, and a fearful battle ensued all over the
sacred precincts. The beak, in great consterna-
tion, rushed into the street to look for assistance,
which was cheerfully refused him by the only man
in sight. After every article of furniture had
been tumbled over about 307 times, the pursued
one, bleeding profusely, managed to escape into
the g irden, where several more rounds were fought
among the flower-beds, while the majesty of the
law stood on the verandah with terror on its
countenance and vainly implored the combatants
to desist. Just then some of the beak’s friends
came past on their way to church, and the battle
was at last cut short; but the magistrate, who
spent the rest of the day in sorting out the debris,
has never been able to convince his shepherd that
he didn’t attend church that Sunday morning
because he was amusing himself by inciting a fight
between two drunken pugilists.

Baroness Burdett-Coutts, in her 81st year,
appears quite likely to outlive the young husband

Country newspaper imprint:—
Printed by J. Callander, junr., and published at hia
office, Tocumwal, N.S.W., in the colony of Victoria.
’E dunno w’ere ’e are !
Dr. Haynes (formerly of Sydney), captain
of Perth (W. A.) cavalry corps, emphatically
denies that he fell from his horse during
■Gov. Kintore’s visit to Perth. The doctor
has evidently developed into a most patriotic
Westralian. “ About the escort,” he writes; “It
is certainly equal to any I have seen in the adjoin-
ing provinces, and although the members be West
Australians they are as fine a body of men as will
be found anywhere, and their mounts are equally
good. There are a mean class of people over here
from the Eastern provinces who delight to ridicule
and libel the West Australian native, but it would
be well for them to imitate and not despise him.
I learned to ride many a long year ago as a
N.S.W. native brought up in the bush.”

“ It is with heartfelt grati-
tude I write to tell you of the
great benefit my little daugh-
ter Gladys has derived from
‘Benger's Food.' The doctor
and all who saw her thought
we should never he able to
rear her. We took her to the

began to pick up flesh and

If every mother knew of
its value no other would be
used.”
A civil-servant of Maryborough (Q.), by
the death of a relative in Great Britain, recently
came into possession of two estates in Wexford,
and valuable property in Regent-street, London.
A woman has applied for a hangman’s
billet in Austria, contending that as woman brings
man into the world she has a right to hoist him
out again. Two wrongs make one right?
It is said that the small veiled female who
has lately stabbed several women in the neighbor-
hood of South Kensington, London, is no other
than a certain titled lady who has been under
restraint for years, and who escaped her keepers.
She has been again secured under lock and key.
Her husband tried long ago to get rid of her, but
for some reason he did not succeed. So a certain
dancer, whom we all know, awaits the natural
course of events to wear a coronet by right.
Dear Bulletin, —Ah.Kow, of Mudgee,
and other correspondents who enthuse over the
geniality, the courtesy and kindness to women, of
the yellow hawker of pestilent “ cabbagee ” and
infected “ letioee,” are as much deceived by the
guileful pagan as those women who buy leprosy-
p ppered vegetables from Sin Fat, because Sin is
s ) suave and deferential, and smiles and giggles,
and babbles in a soft low voice, and is always
squirming in saponaceous humility. Sin Fat is
only meek and crawlsome so long as it suits him,
and can work up a particularly demoniacal scowl
when turned away once or twice from a house
where he was accustomed to do business. I have
seen a Chow hawker, infuriated by such treatment,
stand out upon the road and heap oriental curses
upon the house from door-step to chimney-pot,
with appropriate gesticulation. In any case, Sin’s
g d Ian try and tenderness are nauseous, and the
roughest thing that can be said of the Australian
housewife is that she declines to deal with the
white and wholesome vegetable-hawker because he
will not abase himself before her, and wriggle like
a dung-worm every time she invests in a bunch of
carrots or a peck of peas. The woman does not
expect her butcher to be a fawning sycophant, nor
her grocer, nor draper : why should she ask it of
licr vegetable man? I do not believe she does.
The Australian housewife deals with the Chow
h -cause she is ignorant, and because John comes
handiest. —E.D,

The name of “Shanty” Rooney, a sub-
contractor on one of the early N.S.W. railway
lines, is historic. His method was simple : While
he ran the contract his wife presided over the
attached pub., and he retired worth many thou-
sands. A specimen scene on the weekly pay-day :
“Logan.” “Here Oi am.” “And how much
di ye lave wid th’ ould woman last pay ?” “ Well,
Mr. Rooney, av coorse Oi had a dhrop wid the
bhoys, but there’s an ould father as well’s a woife
and little childher down below.” “Ayah, well
well, there’s yer shtuff, and Oi don’t think Oi can
kape yer goin’ any longer ; sure the job’s about
done, and there’s no more contracts cornin’awhoile
yet. Maguire!” “ Yis, Mr. Rooney.” “And
phwat did ye lave wid th’ould woman?” “Bedad
and Oi left the lot, and Oi never kem away till me
pockets wuz inside out.” “’Tis well, me bhoy,
that yer doin’, and there wuz niver a man on the
job that plazed me more than yer own self.
There’s your pay.” “Ragan, Ragan, anyone see
Martin Ragan ? Oh, yer there, are ye, Martin,
and how does you and the missus stand?” “Faith
it’s on the wrong soideOi’rn thinkin’, Mr. Rooney,
smz haste wake.” “Yis, yis, so she tould me,
but don’t let that trouble ye, there’s your money,
and ye’ll take some min wid ye and titivate up the
fais av the cuttin’, and give an oi to the culvert
beyant. And don’t distriss yeasilf, but see that
the nst does their whack.” Erin-go-bragh !

The landlord was asked by the tenant, who had
built up a good business, if he would object to transfer the
lease, as he was about to sell out. The landlord dis-
claimed any objection, but when seller had signed his
covenant, refused to effect the transfer unless £1 a week
more rent was paid. The vendor had to pay this in a
lump sum to avoid a suit for breach of agreement with
the purchaser. But good old Nemesis has made it right.
The new man went bung, and the shop is empty ; the
landlord was a big investor in a land-bank and increased
his holding by buying more shares with the above lump
sum, and vendor is negotiating with the liquidator to buy
the shop for a song by means of deposit-receipts got
at a big discount.
* * » *
A money-lender, in a recent Melb. interpleader
case, expressed his weariness of business, and on being
by the Judge why he continued it, exclaimed :
veil, I musht employ my mindt, yer Honor.”
• • * »
Some of the big Melb. warehousemen, who re-
quire their hands to work at night during stock-taking
have actually stopped the employes tea-money.

coaching firm lost the Wiloannia-Broken
Hill mail contract. Presurnabty in consequence of this it
is now carrying passengers the 160 miles for 10s. The

Brokeri Hill contract. It is now carrying passengers for
2s. 6d. instead of the old £l. Thus is the little man
smashed.

Some people have no luck at all. A “gent
one,” lately in trouble, formerly prac.isel in another
country, ami while under a cloud a commission to
take evidence in connection with a large commercial dis-
p ite was s»nt to Australia directed to him. Hail he pulled

* * » ♦
Some of the big Southern advertising sewing-
machine firms report “no biz,” and have dismissed
almost all their canvassers. The second-hand machine-
man is becoming an aggressive rival, sewing-machines
being picked up at sales for a song, and, with a little
repair, made equal to new, thus enabling a good machine
to be sold at half the price of a new one. The profits in
the past have been enormous—-a machine costing £4 being
often sold at £l2, time-payment.
* * * *
One pushing, successful and well-conducted
Trustees Co., Ltd., has £lO,OOO in the hands of Govt,
as security. Its biz. (and naturally its liabilities),
having increased considerably, as a matter of public
policy the security should be augmented.
* • ♦ ♦
The big life-companies are now at their wits’
ends to push business, and one big co. is advertising for
“society ladies as canvassers.” The clergy, it may not
be generally known, are large agents in this department
of business.
• » * *
The statistics of Vic. life-assurance are not unin-
teresting. The average duration of accepted lives is 65
years. Female lives are 20 per cent, riskier than male.
The average sum for which people assure is £3OO, for
which they pay about £l2 12s. per annum. The chances
are six to one that you will die in your house against your
house being burnt down. The assets of accumulated
funds equal 15 per cent, of the liabiliti s, the income
equals 5 per cent., and the claims paid 3 per cent.
• » ♦ *
Laborers employed by Govt. on deep-drainage
works in Adelaide complain that the rate per yard paid
for excavation only enables good men to earn 2s. per day.
* * » •
Every man (says a lately-returned New Aus-
tralian) is his own distiller in Paraguay. The spirit,
which is distilled from sugar-cane, is good, cheap, and
pure.

ing lotteries, the placards of the St. Albans distribution
are exhibited at Spencer-street railway station.

The Western Champion ventures to assert that
the Dayspring mine at Parkes, N.S. VV., on £20,000 capital,
will pay 20 per cent, and allow a fair reserve. The mine
has been full of water for years.

“Wentworth Proprietary” has found casting
its own shoes and dies a frost. It gets them of Krupp
steel from Germany now ; and reputes them to last 10
months per set. The ordinary life of hematite is about
three.
* * * *
A Sydney paper which has much boomed the
Bingera (N.S. W.) diamond-mines alleges that “Captain
Rogers, the modern Count of Monte Criato,” so fir
from not having found any diamond over 2 carats in bis
property, came across one of 11 carats 2 grains, “ which he
sold in London for £1000.”

After more weary waiting the prospects of the
Pearl mine, Bendigo, are good for another div. period.

“Take my tip,” said a Westralian visitor in
Sydney, the other day, “ Esperance Bay will be the great
port tor the province. Tle Forrest crowd are doing all
they know to keep it back in the interests of the old-
settled districts. When the goldfields and the goldfields
interests get the balance of power in Parliament—which
they will do in a year or so—you will see wharves at
Esperanee and a railway to Ooolgardie which would bring
the field within tour days of Melbourne.

Dear Bulletin, —Carr-Boyd’s prospecting expe-
dition is quite distinct from Dunn’s. 0.-B. is equipped
by a Wealth of Expectations syndicate-member, and told
to steer whatever course he pleases; while the Dunn
crowd always steer N. by E. The parties are now several
hundred miles asunder. —Yours, A 90-Milee.

Ooolgardie luck : “ The outcrop had been
napped by previous prospectors who had chipped pieces
off an enormous boulder but had not lilted the fractured
junk. This was done by M'Gregor, who found it enor-
mously rich.” Careful M’Gregor ! Later arrival fossicked
around the property and secured 200 oz. in specimens in a
few minutes. Possibly they could not do the same again
in two months or two years.

One who should know avers that the present
infatuation of the bull-headed British investor for
Westralia has been without parallel since the days of the
■ South Sea bubble. Stockbrokers’ clerks are working
double-shifts in London, and even the principal’s settle-
ment of affairs for the day is deferred perforce till two or
three in the morning.
The haymakers in this sunshine will be un-
limited, and the sterility of their field of operations for
the future an absolute certainly. J. Bull is either lavish
or impenetrable : discriminating—never.
At morn the boomer boometh
Athwart the curt kerbstone ;
At midnight the moon cometh
And looketh down alone.

The sensations wired nearly every week from
Coolgardie have had little effect on the public and less on
the gold-returns from that field. It will be a hideous
nightmare to the London boomers when the returns of the
current quarter are known.
* • * »
Waihi(M.L.) mine—worth, at present market-
rate, nearly a million sterling—was worked unprofitably
for 10 years, and was at last purchased for a few hundreds
by the present co. The ore is very refractory, and the
old battery process saved barely 30 percent. Cyanide
saves over 90 per cent.
♦ ♦ » «
The Talisman mine, Kuranguhake (M.L.), is re-
ported to be a second Waihi. It is in the same district.
♦ * * *
Three Thames (M.L.) miners, Male. Bruce,
Sandy Bruce, and Styak, had a queer experience lately.
They struck a specimen leader in their drive and followed
it for over 100 ft., golden stone all the Way. Male. Bruce
was offered £BOOO for his share, but his mates refused to
let him sell. The leading stope proved that the shoot of
gold didn’t go up, and a little picking along the floor
showed that it didn’t go down—and so a mine, apparently
good value for £20,000 or £30,000, proved to be worth not
so many hundreds. The moral is ancient. Many a mine
has been spoilt with a pick.
» ♦ * *
Dear Bulletin, —A tight place. We were
crosscutting at the bottom of an 80ft. shaft which was
nicely shaded by a large gum-tree 20ft. away. I being at
windlass, Jack down below', and crib-time having arrived,
I signalled him up, and, receiving the “All right,”
started to wind. The morning had been windy, and just
when I was expecting to see Jack’s head heave in sight,
an extra-strong blast knocked over the above-mentioned
tree. I viewed the falling mast, and then, with great pre-
sence of mind, bolted —deciding to let Jake take his
chance. The tree struck the windlass-barrel and sent the
w'hole concern down the shaft. I listened for the dull,
sickening thud and heard it—also, various other noises,
profane mostly. Bat Jack wasn’t hurt a bit —not
being in a hurry for lunch he had filled the bucket with
quartz, which, being rich in mineral, seemed to me quite
as heavy as a man at the end of the rope.—Golden Bugle,
Coolgardie.

On the Plains ,
(MAORILAND).
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Alone with the silence, the sun and sky.
Alone on the tussocky plain I lie.
An ocean of yellow from East to West
Still rolling and sweeping, far crest on crest;
And billow on billow the tussocks bend
Until in one shimmering haze they blend ;
Where, under the distance, the heat and noon,
The plains, in an ecstacy thrilling, swoon
And melt in the yellow-tinged sombre air,
Like perfume from roses on evenings rare !
Where the sky and the misty horizon meet
The flax-bushes float like a far-off fleet.
And slowly th«y swim with no spray nor splash,
While swell their green sails and their brown oars
flash !
So, lost in two oceans —of plain and sky—
Full-length on the tussocks alone I lie!
Maoriland.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.

Lady Herbert, in the Dublin Review,
after a trip to Moscow, states that the foundling
hospital there has 14,000 babies, and 600 wet
nurses. No Yarra, no Coroner Ghoul, no Mrs.
Grurulikoff, no Madame Herod.
Parson Cook, the new M.L. evangelist,
believes that hell will be a place of perpetual
solitary contiuement where every sinner will sit
down for billions of aeons and think—think —
think incessantly, and never do anything but
think, save that perhaps once in a dozen glacial
periods or so he will shuffle his feet and emit a
hollow groan, an 1 between the shuffles of his feet
a dozen successive solar systems will grow old and
die. The church has a fine, picturesque taste for
infinite horrors.
Queen Victoria has abandoned the habit
. . ... . . c , ■ , .
of walking without support ot one kind or other,
therefore she will never more figure in an indoors
State procession. When a queen processes she must
travel strictly “on her own.” It wouldn’t seem
royal for her to be supported on either side, or
held up from the rear, nor is there any precedent
r iji.. i i • j p
for a lady monarch getting along by the aid of a
thick stick in public at least not in ofcate. V.B.
as a pedestrian spectacle is positively a thing of
the past, but, thank Heaven ! Hingland is Hing-
land still.

In a recent Hall Mall Budget article
on Electric Telegraphs, it is stated that “in
South Kensington Museum may be seen the iden-
tical instrument by means of which the murderer
who escaped from Paddington by train was
arrested at Slough.” All wrong. It was in 1845,
and the murderer, John Taw ell, once a philan-
thropic Quaker chemist in Sydney, after giving
his mistress prussic acid at Saltash (the house was
standing three years ago), near Slough, went by
train to Paddington and was met there by a
detective, who went in the same omnibus with
him to the Jeru-alem coffee-house which Tawell
was m the habit of frequenting, and there arrested
him.

ELLIOTT BROS., LIMITED, O’Connell Street Sydney.
Starting the Selection .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It’s twenty years ago now since we settled on the
Creek. Twenty years ! I remember well the day
we came from Stanthorpe, on Jerome’* dray—
eight of us, and all the things—beds, tubs, a
bucket, the two cedar chairs with the pine bottoms
and backs that Dad put in them, some pint-pots
and old Crib. It was a scorching hot day, too —
talk about thirst! At every creek we came to we
drank till it stopped running.
Dad didn’t travel up with us : he had gone some
months before, to pat up the house and dig a
waterhole. It was a slabbed house, with shingled
roof, and space enough for two rooms ; bat the
partition wasn’t up. The floor was earth ; but
Dad had a mixture of sand and fresh cow-dung,
which housed to keep it level. About once every
month he would put it on; and everyone had to
keep outside that day till it was dry. There were
no looks on the doors : pegs were put in to keep
them fast at night; and the slabs were not very
close together, for we could easily see through
them a person coming on horseback. Joe and I
used to play at counting the stars through the
cracks in the roof.
The day after we arrived Dad took mother and
us out to see the paddock and the flat on the other
side of the gully that he was going to clear for
cultivation. There was no fence round the pad-
dock, but he pointed out on a tree the surveyor’s
marks, showing the boundary of our ground. It
must have been fine land, the way Dad talked
about it! There was very valuable timber on it,
too, so he said ; and he showed us a place, among
some rocks on a ridge, where he was sure gold
would be found, but we weren’t to say anything
about it. Joe and I went back that evening and
turned over every stone on the ridge, but didn’t
find any gold.
No mistake, it was a real wilderness —nothing
but trees, “goannas,” dead timber, and bears;
and the nearest house—Dwyer’s—was three miles
away. I often wonder how the women stood it
the first few years; and I can remember how
mother, when she was alone, used to sit on a log,
where the lane is now, and cry for hours. Lonely !
It was lonely.
Dad soon talked about clearing a couple of acres
and putting in corn—all of us did, in fact—till the
work commenced. It was a delightful topic before
we started ; but in two weeks the clusters of fires
that illumined the whooping bush in the night,
and the crash upon crash of the big trees as they
fell, had lost all their poetry.
We toiled and toiled clearing those four acres,
where the haystacks are now standing, till every
tree and sapling that grew there was down. We
thought then the worst was over ; but how much
we knew of clearing land ! Dad was never tired
of calculating and telling us how much the crop
would fetch if the ground could only be got ready
in time to put it in ; so we labored the harder.
Witli our combined male and female forces and
the aid of a sapling lever we rolled the thundering
big logs together in the face of Hell’s own fires;
and wiien there were no logs to roll it was tramp,
tramp, the day through, gathering armfuls of
sticks, while the clothes clung to our backs with a
muddy.perspiration. Sometimes Dan and Dave
(they were the oldest—17 and 15) would sit in the
shade beside the billy of water and gaze at the
small patch that had taken so long to do ; then
they would turn hopelessly to what was before
t hem and ask Dad (who would never take a spell’
What was the use of thinking of ever getting
such a place cleared ?” And when Dave
wanted to know why Dad didn’t take up a place
on the plain, where there were no trees to
grub and plenty of water, Dad would cough as if
something was sticking in his throat, and then
curse terribly about the squatters and political
jobbery. He would soon cool down, though, and
get hopeful again.
“Look at the Dwyers,” he’d say; “from ten
acres of wheat they got .£7O, last year, besides
feed for the fowls; they’ve got corn in now, and
there’s only the two.”
It wasn’t only burning-off ! Whenever there
came a short drought the water-hole was sure to
run dry ; then it was take turns to carry water
from the springs—about two miles. We had no
draught-horse, and if we had there was neither
water-cask, trolly, nor dray ; so we humped it—
and talk about a drag ! By the time you returned,
if you hadn’t drained the bucket, in spite of the
big drink you’d take before leaving the springs,
more than half would certainly be spilt through
the vessel bumping against your leg every time
you stumbled in the long grass. Somehow, none
of us liked carrying water. We would sooner
keep the fires going all day without dinner than
do a trip to the springs.
One hot, thirsty day it was Joe’s turn with the
bucket, and he managed to get back without
spilling very much. We were all pleased because
there was enough left after the tea had been made
to give each a drink. Dinner was nearly over;
Dan had finished, and was taking it easy on the
sofa, when Joe said :
“I say, Dad, what’s a nater-dog like?” Dad
told him. “ ifellow, sharp ears and bushy tail.”
“Those muster bin some then thet I seen—l
don’t know ’bout the bushy tail—all th’ hair had
corned off.” “ Where’d y’ see them, Joe?” we
asked. “ Down ’n th’ springs floating about—
dead.”
Then everyone seemed to think hard and look
at the tea. I didn’t want any more. Dan
jumped off the sofa and went outside; and Dad
looked after mother.
At last the four acres—excepting the big-
gest of the iron-bark trees and about fifty stumps
were pretty well cleared; and then came a problem
that couldn’t be worked-out on a draught-board.
I have already said we hadn’t any draught horses ;
indeed, the only thing on the selection like a horse
was an old “ tuppy ” mare that Dad used to
straddle. The date of her foaling went further
back than Dad’s, I believe; and she was
shaped something like an alderman. We found
her one day in about 18 inches of mud, with both
eyes picked out by the crows, and her hide bearing
evidence that a feathery trib3 had made a roost of
her carcase. Plainly, there was no chance of
breaking up the ground with her help. We had
no plough, either ; how then was the corn to be
put in ? That was the question.
Dan and Dave sat outside in the corner of the
chimney, both scratching the ground with a chip
and not saying anything. Dad and mother sat
inside talking it over. Sometimes Dad would get
up and walk round the room shaking his head ;
then he would kick old Crib for lying under the
table. At last mother struck something which
brightened him up, and he called Dave.
“ Catch Topsy and ” He paused because he
remembered the old mare was dead.
“Run over and ask Mister Dwyer to lend me
three hoes.”
Dave went; Dwyer lent the hoes ; and the prob-
lem was solved. That was how we started.
Brisbane.
STEELE RUDD.
“ Idle Singers of an Empty Day.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Must a poet sing in the days that are here
Only of moons and roses,
And lips, and limbs of a last loved dear
Pictured in pretty poses?
Is there nothing then that a man may sing,
Who singebh in those last ages,
But themes that are worn, or scenes that are torn
Out of the dead Past’s pages ?
They are gone—your Arthur and Guinevere —
Gone from the world for ever ;
If ever they were, they are not now here —
Songs can recall them never.
The cruel, cold Past is gone at last.
Laden with sin and sorrow;
What serve your wails and your old-world tales ?
Sing of To-day and To-morrow.
This iron age, with its iron life,
Sombre with sweat and curses,
And the ominous stillness of pending strife,
Hath it no theme for verses ?
And the day new-born, and the light of the morn,
Breaking on brightened faces,
And righted wrongs—herein are songs
Better than languid graces.
Poets, the voices of dawn are heard I
Loudly announce the morning ;
Now is the fight so long deferred,
Sound ye the battle warning.
Sing victory won, and long strife done,
Unto each man that liveth ;
Sing life in the light, and equal right
To all that the good world giveth.
Sydney.
J.P.P.

BIRTH. —Mis. . Hard luck! ninth child,
born March 1, ’95, and wheat only two bob.
Melb. City Council has accepted a contract
for carting at the rate of ss. Bd. a day for a man,
horse, and dray ! The driver gets 3s. 4d. per day
if he has the luck to be employed full time. The
anti-sweating movement in Melbourne is ap-
parently dead.
A Yic. miner, a decent, sturdy young
fellow, got married not long ago, and found that
the gilt soon left his gingerbread. One thing, the
lady was lazy—horribly lazy. Husband, after
waiting five weeks for washing-day, thought he
would start himself, and shame her. “ Why,
whatever are you doing ? ” she asked, coming out
to find him buried in suds. “Just washing a few
of my things,” he answered curtly. “O you
dear,” replied his charmer, “ wait a minute, and
I’ll get you some of mine.” The man said
nothing, but gritted his teeth and got through the
job. Next day, wife announced her intention of
going to spend a fortnight with her mother—
“thought a change would do her good.” Hus-
band agreed, and saw her off cheerfully. Wife
came back: found furniture sold, husband gone,
another tenant in the cottage. All her distress
yielded no clue : he had disappeared utterly.
Wife back with mother again—sadder, wiser.
Cremation epitaph : “ Well done.”
London Morning Leader, a daily
paper, has joined the vast army of the wholesale
Bulletin-par-stealers-without-acknowledgment.
Superintendent “Nick Larkins,” who,
when doing street-duty in Sydney with “his
wife,” Sergeant Mulqueeny, knew every thief by
sight, was, on his promotion, sent out back, a
most uncongenial position. It is now stated that
he is returning to Sydney to succeed Super.
Oamphin at the Detective Office, a post for which
he should be eminently fitted. Camphin retires
on pension after long and distinguished services.
A tremendous “reform” in Victorian
railway management is heralded by three-stick
pars in the local press. In future, with a view to
the economy of power and fuel, engine-drivers are
to avail themselves of momentum in climbing
high grades. “The use of momentum,” says the
Age, “is now being thoroughly applied.”" The
engine-driver now rushes a hill that he formerly
approached with diffidence at a two-raile-an-hour
gait. In effecting this reform “the departmental
authorities are acting on the scientific basis that a
moving train is in itself a reservoir of force that
can be utilised in supplementing the active motive
power.” The world does move.
Thomas Kelly, known to Sydney larrikins
as “ Old Dad,” died some weeks ago, and now the
City of the B. Harbor mourns the death of
another Kelly, alias “ Paddy the Ram,” the well-
known dumpy little sightless old man who sold
candles on the cab-ranks. “Old Dad” came to
Australia nearly 40 years ago and got a decent
C.S. messengers hi p; “Paddy” arrived “in
bond ” as far back, he asserted, as Gov. Darling’s
time; and his story was that, hearing Australia
was such a fine place and not having the money to
emigrate, he just smashed a jeweller’s window in
Limerick city, stole nothing—and got seven years
for his trouble. He averred that he had personal
cognisance of all the details of the Sudds and
Thompson episode over which Darling was re-
called.
Dear Bulletin, —Lindsey Cooper (The
Bulletin states), night overseer of S. A. Register,
recently retired from that paper after 56 years’
service. As a matter of fact, his actual service
extended over only 50 years, and it is just 55 years
since he joined the paper. He did not come out to
S.A. with the first proprietor, Robert Thomas,
and did not assist to bring out the first number in
a tent in Holdfast Bay. When the paper was
printed under primitive conditions in ’37, Cooper
was, perhaps, shrinking from the dominie’s cane.
He came out here as a small boy, and in 1840 he
was apprenticed to Robert Thomas, being then a
youngster of 13. The first number of The Regis-
ter, with which Mr. Cooper was for so long
honorably connected, was published in London in
1836, and the second issue took place a year later
in Adelaide, not at Holdfast Bay.—Yours, &0.,
An Adelaide Man.
